وقوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم : من أسلف فليسلف في كيل معلوم أو وزن معلوم إلى أجل معلوم ولا خلف في السلم (الحال) فتغير حكم الأصل، وهو جواز السلم مع الخلف إلى جوازه ،بدونه وبعبارة أخرى هذا القياس بغير اشتراط الأجل الثابت بالنص إلى الجواز بدونه ، وهذا ظاهر جداً (وأما النقض على الحنفية بدفع القيمة في الزكاة) وهو تغيير لحكم الأصل الذي هو إيجاب صورة الشاة والصرف) عطف على الدفع أي والنقض بصرف الزكاة إلى صنف واحد، وفيه تغيير لحكم الأصل، وهو كونها ملكاً للأصناف كلها كما يدل عليه اللام (فقد مر دفعه) في فصل التأويل، ثم لما كان ينقض بأن الشيخين قاسا المائع على الماء في إزالة النجاسة مع أن فيه تغيير الحكم النص وهو إيجاب استعمال الماء في تطهير الثياب، فأجاب عنه بقوله : (وأما إلحاق كل مائع طاهر) كالخل وماء الورد وغيرهما (بالماء) في زوال نجاسة الثياب (فللعلم بأن المقصود من قوله صلى الله عليه
Among them—meaning among the conditions—for the branch, as in rulings, is that its illa equal the illa of the original, i.e., the illa is the illa of the original in what equality is intended, either in essence of the illa (like nabidh and wine, they share in intoxicating potency, which is exactly the same in both, even if they differ in strength or weakness, for equality in quantity is not intended) or in genus of the illa (like the limbs [as branch] and the self [as original] regarding retaliation due to the shared jinaya, and jinaya is a genus). Similarly, equality in the ruling is required, either in essence (like killing with a heavy object is measured against killing with a sharp instrument in retaliation, by the common factor of intentional murder and hostility according to the two, so the essence of the ruling transfers to the branch, which is retaliation of life) or in genus (like guardianship over a minor girl in her marriage is measured against guardianship over her property by the common factor of minority, so the same guardianship transfers to the branch, which includes under it guardianship of property and guardianship of self). I say: The meaning of the illa being a genus is that by its generality it necessitates a more general ruling than that in the branch and the original. When it varies according to the variation of the locus, it necessitates in each locus a type of ruling appropriate to it, like jinaya (by its generality necessitates equality, which in the case of life is killing, and in the case of a limb is amputation). So it varies in each and necessitates an appropriate type of ruling. In the case of essential illa, it necessitates a ruling with no difference except in number, by consideration of the locus only, and its variation is not possible, nor variation of the ruling. So the objection in al-Tahrir—that the illa in qiyas is only the exact what was reasoned with for the original's ruling, so equality is only in the essence of the illa, and if what is in the branch is a genus of what was reasoned with for the original, it would be part of the illa, so the illa would not exist in the branch—is refuted; the refutation is clear. Similarly the discussion about the ruling, question and answer: The question is that what exists in the branch is the essence of the original's ruling; if it were a genus, it would be part of the ruling. The answer is that the meaning of the ruling being a genus is that it varies according to the multiplicity of loci. So understand. Among them—i.e., among the conditions of the branch—is that it should not change in the ruling of the original, like the Shafi'i's qiyas of the dhimmi's zihar to that of a Muslim, imposing prohibition while in the original it is terminable by kaffarah (by text), but in the branch it is permanent (not terminable by kaffarah), unlike a slave who is eligible for it but unable, like a pauper. The verification: Kaffarah is an act of worship covering sin; the disbeliever is not eligible for it, because he either performs it in disbelief (impossible) or after Islam, but Islam erases previous sins, so no covering. Thus, if zihar from him were established, it would impose prohibition ending with Islam, not with kaffarah, whereas in the original it ended with kaffarah. This is unlike a pauper unable to fast or a slave unable, for they are eligible; if the pauper becomes rich or the slave is freed and wealthy, monetary kaffarah is valid from them; if their inability to fast ends, expiation by fasting is valid. By this exposition, what is said—that it depends on the disbeliever not being obligated with branches, whereas the prevailing school is that he is obligated, and then he is like a pauper unable to fast—is refuted, for each is obligated for kaffarah but unable to perform. So understand. Then remaining speech: Prohibition in a Muslim is also not permanent; kaffarah is a relief. The one who extends [the ruling] to the dhimmi transfers the same prohibition found in the Muslim, the only difference being that the relief is Islam only. The answer: It is narrated in the four Sunan from Ibn Abbas that a man made zihar on his wife then had intercourse before expiating; the Prophet said: 'What made you do this?' He said: 'I saw her anklet in the moonlight.' He said: 'Separate from her until you expiate.' This specifies prohibition until expiation. And His statement: 'Those who make zihar among you of their wives, then return to what they said, [the penalty is] freeing a slave before they touch each other' [58:3] indicates that for anyone whose zihar is valid, its effect is freeing a slave, etc. So understand. And like his statement—i.e., like the Shafi'i's statement—'salam spot as deferred salam'—both are for removing hardship, so it is valid, although the specified term in salam is a substitute for the required ownership and ability in the sale by text (the prohibition of selling what one does not possess, and the caliph's acknowledgment of dropping them with mention of the term). As in His statement: 'O you who believe, when you contract a debt for a specified term, write it down' [2:282] and his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever gives a loan, let him give a loan with a known measure or known weight to a known term.' There is no substitute in current salam. So the ruling of the original—the permissibility of salam with a substitute—changed to permissibility without it. In other words, this is qiyas without the term condition established by text to permissibility without it, and this is very clear. As for the objection against the Hanafis regarding paying value in zakat—which changes the original's ruling of imposing a specific sheep in form and distribution—and the objection regarding paying zakat to one category—changing the ruling that it is owned by all categories as indicated by the lam—its refutation has already been mentioned in the chapter on ta'wil. Then some object that the two sheikhs measured liquid pure [substances] on water in removing impurity, though it involves changing the textual ruling requiring use of water for purifying clothes. He answered: 'As for attaching every pure liquid (like vinegar, rose water, etc.) to water in removing impurity from clothes, it is because it is known that the intent of his statement (peace be upon him) ...'
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage elaborates on three conditions for the branch (far‘) in qiyas. First, the illa of the branch must equal the illa of the original either in essence (e.g., intoxicating potency for nabidh and wine) or in genus (e.g., the genus of jinaya covering both murder and amputation). Second, the hukm must also be equal—again either in essence (retaliation for killing) or in genus (guardianship type). The author clarifies that even if the illa or hukm is a genus, it still fulfills the condition because the genus necessitates a ruling appropriate to each locus. Third, the hukm in the branch must not change in nature relative to the original. This is demonstrated through two Shafi'i analogies that the Hanafis reject: (a) qiyas of dhimmi's zihar to a Muslim's zihar—the original zihar prohibits intercourse until expiation (kaffarah), but since a dhimmi cannot validly perform kaffarah (either in disbelief or after Islam which erases sins), the prohibition would become permanent, changing the nature of the ruling. The Hanafi response cites the hadith and Quran to show that zihar always leads to expiation, which is impossible for a dhimmi, so the qiyas fails. (b) Qiyas of spot salam to deferred salam—the original deferred salam is permissible because the term substitutes for ownership/ability; spot salam lacks this substitute, so the qiyas changes the basis. The author then anticipates objections against Hanafi positions (paying value in zakat, paying one category, and analogizing pure liquids to water) and briefly notes their refutation. The passage emphasizes that qiyas must preserve the essential nature of the original's hukm.

Key Terms
العلة عيناً وجنساً (al-‘illah ‘aynan wa jinsan): The illa either in its exact essence or as a broader genus that encompasses multiple specifics.
تغيير حكم الأصل (taghyīr hukm al-aṣl): Changing the nature or essential characteristic of the original's ruling when transferring to the branch.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, why is the Shafi'i qiyas of a dhimmi's zihar on a Muslim's zihar invalid?
A) Because the dhimmi is not a Muslim.
B) Because the prohibition in the original is temporary (terminable by kaffarah), but in the branch it becomes permanent since the dhimmi cannot perform kaffarah.
C) Because the Quran does not mention dhimmis.
D) Because the hadith says 'five are killed'.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, qiyas is valid only if the branch's illa and hukm equal the original's (in essence or genus) and the hukm's nature remains unchanged.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
12 min
The Condition of Branch Not Changing the Original's Ruling in Qiyas
Explains a condition for qiyas with two Shafi'i counterexamples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition that the branch (far') must not alter the ruling of the original (asl) to evaluate the validity of a qiyas, and explain why the Shafi'i analogies in zihar and salam are rejected.

Core Idea
A valid qiyas preserves the original's ruling; if the branch changes it, the analogy fails.

This passage discusses a fundamental rule for qiyas: the branch case must not modify the essential character of the original ruling. This condition ensures that analogy does not produce a result contradicting the established text. We will examine two Shafi'i analogies that the Hanafi school argues violate this condition and why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for valid qiyas
Read the opening of the passage: 'Among the conditions of the branch is that it should not change in the ruling of the original.' This is the core rule. Understand that any analogy that alters the original's hukm (e.g., making a temporary prohibition permanent) is invalid.
The condition ensures the 'illah (effective cause) is preserved consistently.
2
Analyze the zihar analogy
Compare the original (Muslim zihar) with the branch (dhimni zihar). The original ruling: prohibition is terminable by expiation (kafarah). The branch: dhimni cannot expiate, so prohibition becomes permanent. This changes the original's characteristic of terminability.
The passage contrasts with a slave or poor person who can expiate in the future.
3
Evaluate the Shafi'i objection and Hanafi response
The Shafi'i argue that a dhimni is mukallaf and can expiate after conversion. The Hanafi rebut that expiation is an act of worship covering sin; Islam wipes out prior sins, so expiation post-conversion is meaningless. Thus the dhimni is like a poor person incapable of fasting: both owe expiation but cannot perform it.
The Hanafi cite the hadith of Ibn Abbas linking prohibition to expiation.
4
Analyze the salam analogy
Compare original salam (deferred delivery) with spot salam. Original ruling: salam requires a specified term as a substitute for possession/ability (based on texts). Spot salam omits the term, changing the ruling from permissible with condition to permissible without condition.
The passage notes that the Shafi'i argue both are for removing hardship, but the condition for validity is dropped.
5
Conclude on the validity of each qiyas
Both analogies violate the condition because they change the essential characteristic of the original ruling. Therefore, in Hanafi usul, these qiyas are invalid. The Shafi'i positions are rejected.
This condition prevents analogy from overriding explicit texts (nass).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ومنها) أي من شروط الفرع أن لا يتغير في حكم الأصل كالشافعي) أي كقياس الشافعي ظهار الذمي كالمسلم أي كظهاره (فيوجب الحرمة مع أنها في الأصل متناهية بالكفارة) بالنص (وهي في الفرع مؤبدة) غير متناهية بالكفارة (بخلاف العبد فإنه أهل لها لكنه عاجز كالفقير) وتحقيقه أن الكفارة عبادة ساترة للذنب والكافر ليس أهلا لها أي لأدائها، لأنه إما أن يؤدي حال الكفر وهو لا يمكن لأن الكفر مانع عن أداء العبادات أو بعده بأن يسلم فيؤدّي، وذا لأن الإسلام يهدم الذنوب السابقة، فلا ستر فإذن لا يمكن أداء الكفارة، فلو ثبت الظهار منه لأثبت الحرمة المنتهية إلى الإسلام لا إلى الكفارة وقد كانت في الأصل منتهية إليها، وهذا بخلاف الفقير العاجز عن الصيام والعبد العاجز، فإنهما أهلان للكفارة، كيف ولو صار الفقير غنياً والعبد معتقاً موسراً صح منهما الكفارة المالية أو زال عجزهما عن الصوم صح التكفير بالصيام وبتقريرنا هذا اندفع ما قيل : إنه متوقف على كون الكافر غير مكلف بالفروع والمذهب المنصور أنه مكلف وحينئذ مثله كمثل الفقير العاجز عن الصيام بعينه، فإن كلا منهما مما وجب الكفارة عليه، لكنهما غير قادرين على الأداء فافهم، ثم بقي كلام هو الحرمة في المسلم غير متناهية أيضاً، إنما الكفارة مخلص والمعدي في الذمي نفس الحرمة الموجودة في المسلم غاية ما في الباب أن المخلص فيه الإسلام لا غير. والجواب : «إنه روي في السنن الأربعة عن ابن عباس أن رجلاً ظاهر من امرأته ثم وقع عليها قبل أن يكفر فقال : عليه السلام ما حملك على هذا؟ قال رأيت خلخالها في ضوء القمر فقال: «فاعتزلها حتى تكفر» وفيه تنصيص على الحرمة إلى التكفير ، وقوله تعالى: وَالَّذِينَ يُظْهِرُونَ مِن نِّسَآبِهِمْ ثُمَّ يَعُودُونَ لِمَا قَالُوا فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مِّن قَبْلِ أَن يَتَمَاسَاً ﴾ [المجادلة : ٣] يدل على أن كل من يصح ظهاره، فأثره تحرير رقبة الخ فافهم (وكقوله) أي كقول الشافعي (السلم الحال كالمؤجل) فإن كليهما لدفع الحرج فيصح مع أن الأجل المنصوص في السلم خلف عن الملك والقدرة الواجبين) في المبيع (بالنص) وهو النهي عن بيع ما ليس عند الإنسان وعرف الخليفة بإسقاطهما مع ذكر أن الأجل، كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا إِذَا تَدَايَنتُم بِدَيْنِ إِلَى أَجَلٍ مُّسَمًّى فَاكْتُبُوهُ [البقرة: ۲۸۲] وقوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم : من أسلف فليسلف في كيل معلوم أو وزن معلوم إلى أجل معلوم ولا خلف في السلم (الحال) فتغير حكم الأصل، وهو جواز السلم مع الخلف إلى جوازه بدونه وبعبارة أخرى هذا القياس بغير اشتراط الأجل الثابت بالنص إلى الجواز بدونه ، وهذا ظاهر جداً
Among the conditions of the branch is that it should not change in the ruling of the original—like the Shafi'i, i.e., the Shafi'i's analogy of a dhimmi's zihar to a Muslim's zihar, thus he imposes permanence while in the original it is terminable by expiation, according to the text, while in the branch it is permanent. Unlike the slave, who is eligible for it but incapable like the poor. The investigation: expiation is an act of worship covering the sin, and the disbeliever is not eligible for it, either performing it in disbelief (impossible because disbelief prevents acts of worship) or after embracing Islam, but Islam wipes out previous sins, so no covering. Thus expiation cannot be performed. If zihar were established from him, it would establish a permanence ending at Islam, not expiation. This differs from the poor incapable of fasting and the incapable slave, as they are eligible for expiation. Even if the poor becomes rich and the slave becomes free and wealthy, they can perform financial expiation, or if their incapacity for fasting ends, they can expiate by fasting. With this repudiation is rebutted the objection that it depends on the disbeliever not being obligated by religious rulings, while the supported view is that he is obligated. In that case, he is similar to the poor incapable of fasting: both have expiation incumbent but are unable to perform. Understand. Then there remains the point: the prohibition in the Muslim is also not terminable; expiation is a way out, and what is transmitted in the dhimmi is the same prohibition; the maximum is that the way out is only Islam. The answer: it is narrated in the four Sunan from Ibn Abbas that a man made zihar from his wife then had intercourse before expiation; the Prophet said: 'What made you do this?' He said: 'I saw her anklet in moonlight.' The Prophet said: 'Separate from her until you expiate.' In this is explicit ruling of prohibition until expiation. And Allah's saying: 'And those who pronounce zihar from their wives and then return to what they said, free a slave before they touch one another' (Mujadila:3) indicates that whoever's zihar is valid, its effect is freeing a slave etc. Understand. And like his saying, i.e., the Shafi'i's saying: 'Spot salam is like defered salam', both are for removing hardship, so they are valid, although the specified term in salam is a substitute for ownership and ability required in the sold item, according to the text prohibiting selling what one does not possess, and the Caliph recognized dropping them while mentioning the term, as Allah's saying: 'O you who believe, when you contract a debt for a specified term, write it down' (Baqara:282) and the Prophet's saying: 'Whoever advances salam, let him advance in a known measure or known weight to a known term.' There is no substitute in spot salam; thus the ruling of the original changed, i.e., permissibility of salam with a substitute to permissibility without it. In other words, this is analogy without the condition of term established by text to permissibility without it, and this is very obvious.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage discusses a key condition for the validity of qiyas: the branch (far') must not entail a change in the ruling of the original (asl). The text critiques two Shafi'i analogies that supposedly violate this condition. First, the Shafi'i qiyas of zihar from a dhimni (non-Muslim) to a Muslim: the original ruling for a Muslim is that zihar creates a temporary prohibition terminable by expiation. For a dhimni, expiation is impossible (either during disbelief or after Islam because Islam wipes out prior sins), so the prohibition becomes permanent. This changes the original's essential characteristic (termination by expiation). The Hanafi response defends that the dhimni is mukallaf and similar to a poor person incapable of fasting—both owe expiation but cannot perform it, so the analogy is invalid. Second, the Shafi'i analogy of spot salam (salam with immediate delivery) to deferred salam: the original requires a term as a substitute for possession and ability; dropping the term changes the ruling. The Hanafi critique shows that such analogies violate the condition, thus qiyas is invalid.

Key Terms
الفرع (al-far'): The branch case in qiyas, which is analogized to the original case (al-asl).
الظهار (al-zihar): A form of divorce where a husband compares his wife to his mother, requiring expiation before resuming relations.
السلم الحال (al-salam al-hal): A salam contract where the price is paid immediately but delivery is immediate, without a specified term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school reject the Shafi'i qiyas of spot salam to deferred salam?
A) Because spot salam involves immediate payment and delivery, which is not mentioned in the texts.
B) Because the condition of a specified term is established by text; dropping it changes the original ruling.
C) Because salam is only valid for fungible goods.
D) Because the Shafi'i evidence is weak.

Key Takeaway
A valid qiyas must preserve the original ruling's essential characteristic; if the branch alters it (e.g., making temporary prohibition permanent or dropping a textual condition), the analogy is invalid.

The Hanafi Qiyas of Pure Liquids for Purification
Defends Hanafi qiyas of liquids to water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of ta'lil (rationing the legal cause) to extend the ruling of using water for removing najasa to other pure liquids, and distinguish between najasa and hadath in terms of qiyas applicability.

Core Idea
If the purpose is removal of impurity, the medium is not sacred — any effective remover works.

This passage confronts a criticism: the Hanafi school analogizes pure liquids like vinegar and rose water to water for removing physical impurity (najasa), while the hadith specifically mentions water. The author defends this qiyas by arguing that the prophetic command's purpose is removal, not the medium. Understanding this argument reveals a foundational usul principle: when the Lawgiver's intent is clear (the illa of removal), the ruling extends to all cases sharing that illa, even if the text specifies a particular instance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and its basis
The objector claims that using liquids other than water for purification changes the text's ruling requiring water. Note the objection's assumption: that the specific mention of water implies exclusivity.
This is a common literalist argument; the Hanafi response will challenge it.
2
Extract the Hanafi response from the hadith
The author argues that the Prophet's command to 'wash it with water' has the purpose of removal (izala), and water is mentioned only out of habit (ghalabat al-isti'mal). Identify the key phrase 'المقصود هو الإزالة' (the purpose is removal).
This is a core usul move: distinguishing between the specific example and the underlying cause.
3
Analyze the rational argument: removal of substance removes impurity
The author presents a rational proof: removing the substance of impurity (najasa) automatically removes its attribute (being impure). Therefore, any liquid that physically removes the impurity should be effective.
This is a qiyas jaliy: the illa (removal) is clear and the far' (other liquids) shares it.
4
Address the counter-argument: liquids become impure
The objector says that other liquids become impure upon contact, so they cannot purify. The Hanafi response: the Lawgiver exempts the liquid used for purification from being considered impure during the act, due to necessity (darura). This dispensation extends to all eliminators.
This shows that the dispensation is not unique to water, but rational.
5
Distinguish najasa from hadath
The passage notes that hadath (ritual impurity) is not a tangible substance but a legal state. Its removal is purely devotional (ta'abbudi) and confined to water. Therefore, the qiyas for liquids applies only to najasa, not to hadath.
This distinction limits the scope of the analogy and shows nuanced usul reasoning.

Source Text

وجوده وبالقطع قد زال المحل : وأنت لا يذهب عليك أن مقصود «التحرير» الاستدلال على عدم إيجاب الحديث استعمال الماء، وإلا كان القطع تركاً للواجب وغير مجز بل المقصود الإزالة فيتعدى إلى كل مائع ثم فيه نظر لأنه هب أن المقصود الإزالة، لكن لا يمكن باستعمال المائع، فإن كل ما يلاقي النجاسة يتنجس، فلا يكون مطهراً، وإنما الماء اعتبر طاهراً حين الملاقاة على خلاف القياس، فلا يقاس عليه غيره وأجيب عنه بأن عدم فاقتصر على المنصوص من المزيل) وهو الماء، ولم يتعد إلى غيره لعدم ورود النص
As for the objection against the Hanafis regarding paying the value in zakat, it is a change in the ruling of the original which is the obligation of the form of a sheep, and (the objection regarding) spending zakat on one category, it changes the ruling of the original which is that it is owned by all categories as indicated by the lam, its rebuttal has passed in the chapter on tawil. Then as for the objection that the two shaykhs analogized liquid to water in removing impurity, which changes the text's ruling requiring the use of water for purifying garments, he answered his saying: 'As for attaching every pure liquid like vinegar and rose water etc. to water in removing impurity from garments, it is because the purpose of the Prophet's saying in response to the woman who asked about menstrual blood on her garment: 'Scratch it and wash it with water' narrated by Abu Dawud and in the narration of the two shaykhs: 'When blood from menstruation gets on the garment of one of you, let her pinch it, then pour water over it, then pray in it' is only the removal of impurity, and water is not the intended medium. (I say: because the removal of the substance necessitates the removal of the attribute; when the substance of impurity is removed, its attribute of being impure is removed, so the place becomes pure by its removal. Thus the ruling extends to every remover that is an eliminator like water. There is a point: the removal only occurs after the liquid remover becomes impure; one impurity is removed and another takes its place. And this is better than what is in al-Tahrir of using consensus for sufficiency of cutting the part. It is known that the specific mention of water is just out of habit, as it is commonly used. The discussion here is about purifying the place after it exists; by cutting, the place is removed. And you should not overlook that the purpose of al-Tahrir is to argue against the obligation of using water from the hadith, otherwise cutting would be abandoning what is obligatory and invalid. Rather, the purpose is removal, so it extends to every liquid. Then there is a consideration: even if the purpose is removal, it cannot be achieved by using liquid, because everything that comes into contact with impurity becomes impure, so it cannot be a purifier. Water is considered pure at the time of contact contrary to analogy, so other things cannot be analogized to it. It is answered that not considering it impure is due to the necessity of removal, so this is general for every liquid. If it is meant that other liquids do not remove, that is contradicted by the senses. If it is meant that the Lawgiver does not consider such removal, that is the point of dispute. And it may be established that purification by water is outside the norm of analogy, because it would require that water does not purify at all except running water etc., since whenever it meets the impure garment, it becomes impure and the garment becomes more impure, so it cannot purify. But we have found a conclusive proof indicating purification by water, so we act on it contrary to analogy. Thus purification using water is an act of worship, so other liquids are not analogized to it. Yet one may say: since the Lawgiver considered the use of water as purification, it is known that He did not give water the ruling of impurity at the time of use. This is a rational legal ruling, reasoned by its being an eliminator of impurity, so it extends to all eliminators. So there is no departure from the norm of analogy here. There is no ritual element except that the Lawgiver ordered us to remove impurity and did not give the remover the ruling of impurity. All this is rational. Understand. Then this, i.e., the impure garment, is unlike hadath, for it is not a real thing fixed in the limbs washed in wudu or ghusl, but rather purely ritual. Thus its removal by using water came contrary to analogy, not because it is a remover of an existing thing as in the impure garment. So it is confined to the specified remover, i.e., water, and does not extend to others due to lack of text.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage responds to an objection that the Hanafi qiyas of pure liquids to water for impurity removal changes the original ruling (water specified in hadith). The author first notes that the objection regarding paying value in zakat or spending it on one category has already been refuted. Then he tackles the purification issue head-on. The hadith about menstrual blood mentions water, but the author argues that the prophetic command's purpose is removal of impurity, not the medium itself. Water is mentioned merely because it is common. Since removal of the substance automatically removes the impurity, any liquid that physically removes the impurity should be valid. He addresses a counter-argument: liquids become impure upon contact, so they cannot purify. The answer: the Lawgiver, for purposes of purification, treats the liquid as not being impure during the act (a dispensation). This reasoning is rational and extends to all eliminators. The passage then distinguishes between tangible impurity (najasa) and ritual impurity (hadath): hadath is not an actual substance but a ritual state, so its removal is purely devotional (ta'abbudi) and confined to water. Thus, the analogy for liquids is valid for removing najasa but not for hadath.

Key Terms
المائع الطاهر (al-mai' al-tahir): Any pure liquid that is not najis itself, capable of removing impurity from garments.
النجاسة (al-najasah): Physical impurity (like blood) that can be removed by washing; its removal is by eliminating the substance.
الإزالة (al-izala): The act of removing the physical substance of impurity.
العادة (al-'adah): Habit or common practice; used to argue that water is mentioned out of habit, not as a legal limit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the hadith does not specify water.
B) Because the purpose of the command is removal of impurity, and water is not ritually unique.
C) Because liquids are not considered impure when used for purification.
D) Because the Prophet used other liquids.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses the illa of removal to extend the purification ruling from water to any pure liquid that physically removes najasa, while confining hadath removal to water due to its ta'abbudi nature.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Precedence Condition: Branch Ruling Must Not Precede Original
Usul rule: condition of chronological precedence in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the chronological precedence condition in qiyas to evaluate whether an analogy is valid.

Core Idea
The branch ruling must not precede the original's ruling — chronology matters in qiyas.

In qiyas, a valid analogy requires that the branch's ruling does not pre dated the original's ruling. This condition prevents logical anachronism where a later ruling would be used to impose a requirement on an earlier one. The classic example is the analogy of wudu to tayammum regarding intention: wudu was legislated before Hijra, tayammum after, so one cannot argue that wudu needs intention based on tayammum's later ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Scan the passage for the condition being discussed: 'that its ruling should not precede the ruling of the original'.
This is one of the conditions of the branch (far') in qiyas.
2
Understand the example
The text gives the example of wudu and tayammum: some try to analogize wudu to tayammum in requiring intention because both are forms of ritual purification. But wudu was legislated before tayammum.
The chronological order is established by the Hijra.
3
Analyze the logical problem
If the branch's ruling (intention for wudu) were established, it would exist before its cause (the 'illah of ritual purification that supposedly justifies it from tayammum). This is impossible: an effect cannot precede its cause.
This is the core of the condition.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafis argue that the cause (jami') of 'ritual purification' is not valid because water is naturally cleansing (ma'qul al-ma'na) while earth is purely devotional (ta'abbudi). So no valid analogy exists; the condition is not even triggered.
The condition works as a secondary argument, but the primary Hanafi response is the difference in 'illah.
5
Apply the condition to other cases
Consider any case where a later ruling might be projected backward onto an earlier one. The condition helps reject such analogies as fasid.
E.g., rulings from Madani suwar cannot be analogized to Makki rulings without checking chronology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage discusses another condition of the branch: its ruling must not be chronologically prior to the ruling of the original. The example given is the analogy of wudu to tayammum concerning the obligation of intention (niyyah). The argument is that wudu was legislated before Hijra, while tayammum was legislated later. If one were to analogize wudu to tayammum in requiring intention, the result would be that wudu would need intention based on a cause (its ritual nature) that was established later, which is logically unsound (the cause would postdate its effect). The text then notes that some might use this as an objection against those who differentiate between wudu and tayammum, but it is not effective because the opponent does not accept the common 'illah (ritual purification) as valid. The Hanafis argue that water is naturally cleansing, so it does not require intention, whereas earth is inherently unclean and is made a purifier by legal dispensation (ta'abbud), thus requiring intention. The text cuts off here, but the basic principle is clear: the chronological precedence condition prevents anachronistic qiyas.

Key Terms
تعبدي (ta'abbudi): An act of worship that is purely devotional, its rationale not fully understood by human intellect.
الهجرة (al-Hijra): The migration of the Prophet from Mecca to Medina, a key chronological marker in Islamic legislation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school argue that the condition of precedence is relevant to the wudu/tayammum analogy?
A) Because tayammum was legislated before wudu.
B) Because wudu was legislated before tayammum, so imposing intention from the later ruling on wudu would be anachronistic.
C) Because water is not a natural cleaner.
D) Because intention is not required in any form of purification.

Key Takeaway
The chronological precedence condition in qiyas prevents deriving a ruling for a branch that would have existed before the original's 'illah was legislated, maintaining temporal consistency in legal derivation.

Condition: Far' Ruling Must Not Precede Asl
Explaining a condition of qiyas with rebuttal of an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition that a far' ruling must not precede the asl ruling in qiyas, and evaluate the Hanafi distinction between water and earth in purification.

Core Idea
The ruling of the branch cannot exist before the original – wudu's obligation of intention cannot be analogized from tayammum because wudu was legislated first.

This passage introduces a crucial condition for valid qiyas: the branch ruling must not predate the original. The example of wudu and tayammum demonstrates this principle. The Hanafis further argue that even if temporality were ignored, the commonality (devotional purification) does not hold because water cleans naturally, while earth purifies only by law. We will explore the reasoning and the rebuttal of an opposing argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
State the condition of qiyas discussed: the ruling of the branch (far') must not precede the ruling of the original (asl).
2
Locate the example
What is the example given? Wudu as far', tayammum as asl. Wudu was legislated before Hijrah, tayammum after, so the obligation of intention (niyyah) for wudu via analogy would predate tayammum's ruling.
This violates the condition.
3
Understand the Hanafi argument
Why do Hanafis reject the analogy? They argue water is naturally cleansing, so when used, hadath is removed without intention; earth is inherently unclean and only purifies devotionally when intention is present.
Thus the commonality of 'devotional purification' does not hold.
4
Analyze the objection
The opponent claims both water and earth remove hadath by legal consideration, making them alike. How do Hanafis respond? They deny the similarity: hadath is a legal attribute not a physical impurity, but water's cleansing is natural, so its role differs.
The objection is repelled by pointing out the difference (farq).
5
Evaluate the Quranic evidence
The passage cites Quranic verses to show that water's purification is inherent and necessary, unlike earth's. This supports the Hanafi position that water does not require intention.
The law agrees with nature in water's case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن لا يتقدم حكمه على حكم الأصل كالوضوء أي قياسه على التيمم في وجوب النية بجامع الطهارة التعبدية إذ شرعية الوضوء قبل الهجرة والتيمم بعدها، وذلك لئلا يلزم ثبوته قبل علته التي أوجبت في الأصل. ولو ذكر مثل ذلك إلزاماً على من يفرق بينهما الصح وهذا لا يظهر له وجه فإن العلة التي اعتبرها المستدل ليست صالحة للاعتبار عند الخصم فلا يتوجه الإلزام ويدفع بالفارق كالحنفية يقولون في المثال المذكور إن الماء منظف في نفسه وطبعه فإذا استعمل حصل النظافة وارتفع الحدث، فلا يحتاج إلى النية والتراب ملوث في نفسه شرع مطهراً تعبداً عند إرادة قربة مقصودة لا تصح إلا بالطهارة وبقي في غير هذا الحال على طبعه وهي أي إرادة القربة المقصودة هي النية وما قيل التعدية الرفع المانعية الشرعية التي هي الحدث والماء كالتراب في ذلك فإن كليهما يرفعان تلك المانعية باعتبار الشارع فقط وكون الماء منظفاً طبعاً لا دخل له في هذا الرفع لأن التنظيف إنما يكون في قلع ما جاور، والحدث ليس مجاوراً للمحدث حتى يقلعه، بل هو اعتبار من الشارع، فإزالته أيضاً باعتباره، والماء والتراب سواء فيدفع بمنع المثلية بين الماء والتراب بل الشرع وافق الطبع في الماء كما قال تعالى: ﴿وَيُنَزِّلُ عَلَيْكُم مِّنَ السَّمَاءِ مَاءً لِّيُطَهِّرَكُم بِهِ﴾ [الأنفال: 11] وَأَنزَلْنَا مِنَ السَّمَاءِ مَاءً طَهُورًا [الفرقان: 48] فجعل التطهير لازماً للماء، فكلما استعمل حصل الطهارة والنظافة، بخلاف التراب فإنه ما جعل الطهورية من لوازمه إلا حال إرادة مخصوصة فاتضح الفرق.
And among them is that its ruling should not precede the ruling of the original, like ablution (wudu) — i.e., analogizing it to tayammum regarding the obligation of intention (niyyah) through the commonality of devotional purification, since the legislation of wudu was before the Hijrah and tayammum after it, so that its existence before its cause which necessitated the original would not follow. And if such mention is made as an objection against one who differentiates between them, it is sound. But this has no apparent basis because the cause that the arguer considers is not valid for consideration in the opponent's view, so the objection is not directed and is repelled by the distinguishing factor, like the Hanafis who say in the mentioned example: Water is cleansing in itself and by its nature; so when used, cleanliness occurs and the state of impurity (hadath) is removed, thus it does not need intention. And earth is inherently unclean, legislatively made purifying devotionally when intending a desired act of worship that is only valid with purification, and remains in other than this case in its nature. And 'it'—i.e., intending the desired act of worship—is intention. And what was said: 'The removal (ta'diyah) is the lifting of the legal impediment, which is hadath, and water and earth are alike in that' because both remove that impediment only by the legislator's consideration; and that water is inherently cleansing has no role in this removal because cleansing is only in removing what adheres, and hadath does not adhere to the person in a way that can be removed, rather it is a legal consideration, so its removal is also by His consideration, and water and earth are equal. So it is repelled by denying the similarity between water and earth; rather, the Law agrees with nature in water, as He says: 'And He sends down upon you from the sky water to purify you thereby' and 'And We sent down from the sky pure water.' So He made purification a necessary attribute of water; whenever it is used, purity and cleanliness result, unlike earth, for He did not make purity a necessary attribute of it except in a specific case of intention. Thus the difference is clear.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage discusses a condition of qiyas: the ruling of the far' (branch) must not be temporally prior to the ruling of the asl (original). The example given is wudu and tayammum: wudu was legislated before Hijrah, tayammum after, so one cannot analogize wudu to tayammum regarding the obligation of intention (niyyah) because that would make wudu's ruling (obligation of niyyah) exist before the original (tayammum) itself existed. The passage then addresses an objection: if someone says this reasoning is an ilzam (compelling argument) against those who differentiate between wudu and tayammum, the author notes it is not effective because the opponent may not accept the commonality. The Hanafi position is presented: water is naturally cleansing, so when used it removes hadath without needing intention, while earth is inherently unclean but made purifying by law only for devotional acts. The rebuttal to the opposing view that water and earth are alike in removing hadath is that water's natural cleansing property is irrelevant because hadath is a legal impediment, not a physical impurity. The text concludes by citing Quranic verses to show that the Law agrees with nature in water's purification, unlike earth which only purifies under specific conditions.

Key Terms
الطهارة التعبدية (al-taharah al-ta'abbudiyyah): Purification that is purely devotional, not rational or natural.
المانعية الشرعية (al-mani'iyyah al-shar'iyyah): The legal impediment (hadath) that prevents validity of prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because water is naturally cleansing while earth is inherently unclean
B) Because wudu is obligatory and tayammum is optional
C) Because the verse about water proves it purifies naturally
D) Because hadath is a legal impediment not a physical impurity

Key Takeaway
The condition that the far' ruling must not precede the asl ruling invalidates the analogy between wudu and tayammum for the obligation of intention, and the Hanafis further distinguish based on water's natural cleansing property.

When Qiyas Meets Text: The Condition of the Far' Ruling
Condition: far' ruling not textually established – debate on qiyas utility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position on whether qiyas is permitted when the far' ruling is already established by a text, and distinguish the majority view from that of Fakhr al-Islam and Abu Hashim.

Core Idea
Qiyas can reinforce a textually established ruling; mutual support (ta'āḍud) justifies its use without waste.

In Hanafi usul, qiyas is a powerful tool, but its application is subject to conditions, especially regarding the far' (new case). One key condition debated is whether the far' ruling must be entirely absent from the texts. Fakhr al-Islam argued that if the text already rules, qiyas is wasted. The majority of Hanafis, however, see value in reinforcing the ruling through multiple evidences. Additionally, Abu Hashim al-Mu'tazili proposed that the far' ruling must at least be established summarily, with qiyas providing detail. This lesson unpacks these disputes and why the Hanafi school rejects both restrictive views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
First, note the condition that the far' ruling should not be textually established, neither in negation nor affirmation. Second, note Abu Hashim's condition that the ruling be established summarily (jumlatan) with qiyas providing detail.
These are separate conditions discussed in the passage.
2
Analyze Fakhr al-Islam's position
Fakhr al-Islam and his followers held that if the far' ruling is already established by a positive text, qiyas is wasted because the text is stronger. Understand their reasoning of 'no need and waste'.
They argue qiyas becomes superfluous.
3
Examine the Hanafi majority response
The majority, especially the scholars of Samarqand, objected that qiyas provides mutual support (ta'āḍud) for the evidence, so there is no waste. They permitted qiyas even when the far' ruling is textually established, unless the qiyas adds a ruling that contradicts the text's absoluteness (like naskh).
The text calls this view 'al-ashbah' (more likely).
4
Evaluate the exception
Note the exception: if qiyas adds an additional ruling that contradicts the text's absolute import, it is invalid like abrogation. This resolves the conflict where qiyas would otherwise change the ruling.
Example: if text says 'no expiation' but qiyas imposes it, that would be contradicting.
5
Refute Abu Hashim's condition
Abu Hashim required the far' ruling to be established summarily by text, then qiyas details it. The Hanafi response cites the Companions' analogies on the husband's statement 'You are to me like my mother's back' (zihar), which had no prior textual ruling at all, showing qiyas can establish rulings from scratch.
This refutation relies on the general evidences for qiyas being universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن لا ينص على حكمه لا نفياً، وإلا لم يجز القياس لأن النص مقدم عليه عند المعارضة بالقياس، مثاله قياس الإمام الشافعي كفارة الظهار على كفارة القتل في إيجاب الإيمان، مع أن الإطلاق النص نافٍ إياه. ومنها أن لا ينص على حكمه إثباتاً وإلا ضاع القياس لثبوت الحكم بما هو أقوى منه وهذا الشرط اعتبره الإمام فخر الإسلام ومن في طبقته ومتابعوه واعترض عليه بأن الفائدة التعاضد بين الأدلة فلا ضياع ومن ثمة جوّز الأكثرون القياس مع كون حكم الفرع منصوصاً عليه إثباتاً، ومنهم مشايخ سمرقند رحمهم الله وهو الأشبه. ولعل مراد النافين أنه لا حاجة إذن إلى القياس وحينئذ لا نزاع أصلاً إلا أن يثبت هذا القياس زيادة على النص فإنه مبطل لإطلاق النص كالنسخ فلا يجوز والحق أنه داخل فيما يكون حكمه منصوصاً بالنص المخالف. ومنها لأبي هاشم المعتزلي أن يثبت حكمه جملة والقياس يكون للتفصيل، كحد الخمر يثبت بالحديث من شرب الخمر فاجلدوه وتقديره ثمانين بالقذف أي بالقياس عليه، وهذا القول باطل، فإن أدلة حجية القياس عامة كما سيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى. ورد بأن الأئمة أي الصحابة ومن بعدهم قاسوا قول الزوج أنت علي حرام وهي واقعة متجددة لم يرد فيها لا مجملاً ولا مفصلاً تارة على الطلاق فيقع ثلاثاً كما عن أمير المؤمنين علي وزيد بن ثابت كرم الله وجههما أو واحدة كما عن عبد الله بن مسعود رضي الله تعالى عنه وقاسوا تارة على الظهار فالكفارة واجبة فيه كما عن ابن عباس رضي الله تعالى عنهما وقاسوا تارة على اليمين فيكون إيلاء كما عن الشيخين أمير المؤمنين أفضل الصديقين أبي بكر وأمير المؤمنين عمر وأم المؤمنين عائشة الصديقة رضي الله تعالى عنهم، وفي التيسير عن نص.
And among them (conditions of the far') is that its ruling should not be stipulated by text, neither in negation, otherwise qiyas is not permitted because the text is given precedence over it when they conflict. Example: Imam al-Shafi'i's analogy of the expiation for zihar upon the expiation for killing in requiring faith, while the absolute text negates it. And among them is that its ruling should not be stipulated by text positively, otherwise qiyas would be wasted because the ruling is established by something stronger. This condition was considered by Imam Fakhr al-Islam and those in his class and his followers, and it was objected that the benefit is mutual support of evidences, so there is no waste. Hence the majority permitted qiyas even when the ruling of the far' is positively established by text, among them the scholars of Samarqand, may Allah have mercy on them, and it is more likely. Perhaps the intent of those who deny is that there is no need for qiyas then, and in that case there is no dispute at all; except if this qiyas establishes an addition to the text, for it invalidates the absoluteness of the text like abrogation (naskh), so it is not permitted. The truth is that it falls under what has its ruling stated by a contradictory text. And among them (conditions) from Abu Hashim al-Mu'tazili is that its ruling should be established in summary (jumlatan) and qiyas is for detail, like the hadd punishment for wine: established by the hadith 'Whoever drinks wine, flog him', and its estimation as eighty forms by analogy to qadhf. This statement is false, for the evidences for the validity of qiyas are general as will come, Allah willing. And it is refuted by the fact that the Imams—the Companions and later ones—made analogies on the husband's saying 'You are to me like my mother's back' — a new occurrence not mentioned in text either summarily or in detail — sometimes to divorce so it occurs as three (as from Ali and Zayd), or as one (as from Ibn Mas'ud), and sometimes to zihar so expiation is obligatory (as from Ibn Abbas), and sometimes to an oath so it becomes ila (as from Abu Bakr, Umar, and Aisha), and in al-Taysir from the text.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage discusses two conditions of qiyas regarding the far': (1) its ruling must not be established by a text (nass) in negation or affirmation, and (2) according to Abu Hashim, its ruling must be established summarily, with qiyas providing detail. The first condition is debated: Imam Fakhr al-Islam and his followers hold that if the far' ruling is already in the text positively, qiyas is wasted because the text is stronger. However, the majority (including scholars of Samarqand) argue that qiyas can still be used for mutual support of evidences; thus there is no waste. The exception is if qiyas adds a ruling that contradicts the text's absolutness, which would be like abrogation (naskh) and is invalid. The second condition from Abu Hashim is rejected because the Companions made qiyas on new cases without any textual basis, as seen in the various analogies on the statement 'You are to me like my mother's back' (zihar), which is not mentioned in text at all, showing that qiyas can establish rulings without any prior textual summary.

Key Terms
التعاضد (al-ta'āḍud): Mutual support between different evidences (text and qiyas) strengthening the ruling.
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation of a ruling by a later text; here qiyas that contradicts text's absoluteness is like naskh.
جملة وتفصيل (jumlatan wa tafsil): Summary and detail: Abu Hashim's condition that far' ruling be established summarily by text, then qiyas details it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the majority of Hanafis permit qiyas when the far' ruling is already established by a positive text?
A) Because the text might be weak and needs support.
B) Because qiyas provides mutual support (ta'āḍud) to the evidence.
C) Because the text is only general and qiyas specifies it.
D) Because the text is not considered strong enough to stand alone.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits qiyas to reinforce a textually established far' ruling (except when it contradicts the text), rejecting Fakhr al-Islam's restrictive condition and Abu Hashim's summary-detail model.

Condition of the Branch: Textual Non-Establishment
Stipulating that the branch ruling is not textually established

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition that the branch's ruling should not be textually established, explain the Hanafi majority position allowing qiyas even with textual evidence, and evaluate the opposing view of Fakhr al-Islam.

Core Idea
Analogy is not invalidated by a textual ruling; evidences reinforce each other.

In Hanafi usul, the branch (far') in qiyas must satisfy several conditions. One disputed condition is whether the ruling of the branch must be free from any textual evidence. This lesson explores the dispute between the majority of Hanafis, who allow qiyas even when a text exists, and those who restrict it. Understanding this nuance is crucial for proper application of analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the debated condition
Read the first part of the passage which states that among the conditions of the branch is that its ruling is not textually established, neither negated nor affirmed. Note the immediate exception: if the text negates the ruling, analogy is invalid because the text overrides. If the text affirms, the dispute arises.
Distinguish between 'negated' (nafiyan) and 'affirmed' (ithbatan) textual establishment.
2
Understand Fakhr al-Islam's argument
Fakhr al-Islam and his followers consider that if the ruling is already textually affirmed, analogy would be wasteful (daya') because the ruling is established by something stronger. They view the analogy as redundant.
This is a minority position among Hanafis.
3
Analyze the majority's response
The majority, including the scholars of Samarkand, respond that the benefit of analogy is mutual reinforcement (ta'addud) of evidences. There is no waste; rather, the analogy strengthens the ruling. The text marks this as 'al-ashbah' (the more likely view).
The majority also note that if the negators only meant 'no need', then there is no real dispute—it becomes a semantic issue.
4
Consider the exception of ziyadah
Even the majority agree that if the analogy establishes an addition (ziyadah) to the text—i.e., a ruling that goes beyond what the text says—then it is prohibited like naskh (abrogation). The truth is that such a case falls under what is textually established by a contradictory text.
Ziyadah is invalid because it would contradict the absoluteness (itlaq) of the text.
5
Apply the Companions' practice
The passage provides an example: the Companions analogized the husband's statement 'You are unlawful to me' to divorce, zihar, or oath, even though there was no explicit text for this novel incident. This demonstrates that they used qiyas without a textual basis, supporting the majority view.
The example shows that even in the absence of any text (neither jumla nor tafsil), qiyas is valid.

يقع (واحدة كما عن عبد الله بن مسعود رضي الله تعالى عنه (و) قاسوا تارة على الظهارة فالكفارة واجبة فيه كما) عن ابن عباس رضي الله تعالى عنهما (و) قاسوا (تارة على اليمين) فيكون إيلاء (كما عن (الشيخين أمير المؤمنين أفضل الصديقين أبي بكر وأمير المؤمنين عمر وأم المؤمنين عائشة الصديقة رضي الله تعالى عنهم، وفي «التيسير» عن ابن عباس : إذا قال : هذا الطعام حرام عليَّ ثم أكل فعليه عتاق رقبة أو صيام شهرين متتابعين أو إطعام ستين مسكيناً فعلم أنه ليس عنده ظهاراً ،حقيقة بل هو شبيه به في الكفارة، فافهم (وقد يناقش بأن النص قوله تعالى : ﴿لِمَ تُحَرِّمُ مَا أَحَلَّ اللهُ لَكَ ﴾ [التحريم: ١] الآية وهو يدل على حكم، وأنت حرام إجمالاً وليس منها أي من شروط الفرع القطع بالعلة) أي وجودها (فيه بل ظنية المقدمات كلها كافية فيه (الإيجاب لأن الظن واجب العمل ودعوى الاضمحلال مضمحلة (وأما عدم المعارض المساوي (والراجح) في الفرع ( فإنما هو شرط لإثبات الحكم بالعلة) فيه لا لنفس القياس ( لأن الشهادة لا تزول بالمعارضة) وإنما يتوقف في الحكم فكذا هذا (هذا).
And among them (i.e., conditions of the branch) is that its ruling is not textually stipulated, neither negated, otherwise analogy is not permissible, because the text takes precedence over it when conflicting with analogy. Example: Imam al-Shafi'i's analogy of the expiation of zihar on the expiation of killing in requiring emancipation, while the absolute text negates it. And among them is that its ruling is not textually stipulated affirmatively, otherwise analogy would be wasted because the ruling is established by what is stronger. This condition was deemed valid by Imam Fakhr al-Islam and his peers and followers, and objection was raised against it because the benefit is mutual support of evidences, so no waste. Hence the majority allowed analogy even when the branch's ruling is textually stipulated affirmatively, including the scholars of Samarkand (may Allah have mercy on them). And it is the more likely view. Perhaps the intention of the negators is that there is no need for analogy in that case, and then there is no dispute at all. Unless this analogy establishes an addition to the text, for it would nullify the text's absoluteness like abrogation, so it is not permissible. The truth is that it falls under what is textually stipulated by a contradictory text. And among them (conditions of the branch) according to Abu Hashim al-Mu'tazili is that its ruling is established by text in sum (jumla) and analogy is for detail, like the hadd punishment for wine established by the hadith 'Whoever drinks wine, flog him' and its quantification as eighty (lashes) by analogy with slander. This statement is invalid, for the evidences for the authority of analogy are general. And it is refuted by the fact that the Imams (the Companions and those after them) made analogies in the saying of the husband 'You are unlawful to me' - a novel incident for which there is no text, neither in sum nor in detail - sometimes to divorce, so it counts as three (as from Ali and Zayd ibn Thabit), or as one (as from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud), and sometimes they analogized it to zihar so expiation is obligatory (as from Ibn Abbas), and sometimes to an oath so it becomes a vow of abstinence (as from the two Shaykhs Abu Bakr and Umar, and Aisha). And in al-Taysir from Ibn Abbas: if he says 'This food is unlawful for me' then eats, he must free a slave or fast two consecutive months or feed sixty poor persons. So it is clear that according to him it is not literal zihar but similar in expiation. So understand. And it may be contested that the text is His statement 'Why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful to you?' which indicates a ruling in sum. And the condition that the 'illa (cause) be certain in the branch is not among the conditions of the branch; rather, probabilism in all premises suffices for affirmation because acting on probability is obligatory and the claim of collapse is baseless. As for the absence of an equal or preponderant counterargument in the branch, that is only a condition for establishing the ruling via the 'illa in the branch, not for the analogy itself, because a testimony does not drop due to opposition but the ruling is suspended. Likewise this.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions of the branch (far') in qiyas (analogy) according to Hanafi usul. It first states that the ruling of the branch must not be textually established, either negatively or affirmatively. If it is negatively established, the text overrides the analogy. If affirmatively, analogy would be redundant. This condition was upheld by Fakhr al-Islam and many Hanafi scholars, but the majority (including the scholars of Samarkand) allowed analogy even when the branch's ruling is textually affirmed, because the evidences reinforce each other. However, if the analogy adds something beyond the text, it is like abrogation and invalid. Abu Hashim al-Mu'tazili's condition that the branch's ruling must be textually established in summary (ijmalan) while analogy provides detail is rejected because the authority of analogy is general. The passage then provides examples from the Companions' practice of analogizing the husband's statement 'You are unlawful to me' to various rulings, showing that they used analogy without a textual basis. Finally, it clarifies that certainty (qat') in the 'illa is not required; probability suffices, and the absence of a counterargument is only for establishing the ruling via the 'illa, not for the validity of analogy.

Key Terms
الفرع (al-far'): The branch case in qiyas; the new case whose ruling is sought by analogy.
النص (al-nass): The explicit textual evidence from Quran or Hadith.
الزيادة (al-ziyadah): An addition to the text via qiyas; considered like naskh (abrogation) if it goes beyond the text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars (including the scholars of Samarkand), is it permissible to use qiyas when the ruling of the branch is already established by a textual proof?
A) No, because the text is stronger and analogy becomes redundant.
B) Yes, because evidences can reinforce each other and analogy can provide additional detail.
C) Yes, but only if the text is ambiguous.
D) No, because qiyas cannot be used if there is any text on the matter.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi majority permits qiyas even when the branch's ruling is textually established, because evidences reinforce each other, unless the analogy adds to the text (ziyadah) which is treated like abrogation.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Does Outweighing Harm Invalidate the Appropriateness of a Description?
Dispute on whether a description remains appropriate when it entails a outweighing or equal harm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that a description remains appropriate despite an accompanying harm from a different aspect, and apply this principle to the vow to fast on Eid.

Core Idea
A description stays appropriate if harm is from a different aspect; appropriateness is not destroyed by conflicting considerations.

This passage addresses a subtle usul question: if a description that usually serves as an effective cause for a ruling also entails a harm that outweighs or equals the benefit, does the description lose its appropriateness? The Hanafi school, aligning with Imam al-Razi, argues that the appropriateness remains intact because the benefit and harm arise from different angles (تعدد الجهة). Understanding this principle is crucial for correctly deriving rulings in cases where multiple legal aspects coexist, such as fasting on Eid or prayer in usurped land.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
State the central question: Does a harm that is equal to or greater than the benefit invalidate the description's appropriateness for the ruling? Recognize that there are two levels of invalidation: (1) nullifying the appropriateness itself, and (2) preventing the Lawgiver from considering it.
The first level is considered obviously false by the author; the second is the real point of contention.
2
State the Hanafi position and its rationale
The Hanafis hold that appropriateness is not invalidated. The key proof: there is no contradiction because benefit and harm come from different aspects (تعدد الجهة). Therefore, they can coexist. For example, fasting on Eid has benefit (devotion) from one aspect and harm (turning away from Allah's hospitality) from another.
This is based on rational proof of the impossibility of transformation from appropriate to inappropriate given the coexistence of multiple aspects.
3
Analyze the opposing view and its response
The opposing view (attributed to Ibn al-Hajib and some Shafi'is) claims that appropriateness is invalidated, or at least that the Lawgiver would not consider it. The Hanafi response: the description remains appropriate; the Lawgiver may omit consideration due to the preventer (harm), but the appropriateness itself is not negated.
Imam al-Razi distinguished between nullifying reality and nullifying consideration; the Hanafis accept the latter as possible but not the former.
4
Apply to the Eid fast example
The Hanafis rule that a vow to fast on Eid is valid because from the aspect of devotion the description is appropriate, even though from another aspect the act is prohibited. The Shafi'is would rule it invalid because the harm (prohibition) outweighs the benefit.
This is the practical fruit (ثمرة الخلاف) of the usul dispute.
5
Refine with the usurped land example
The opponent cites prayer in usurped land: the benefit (prayer) is not outweighing the harm (usurpation), yet prayer is valid. The Hanafi answer: the harm is not necessary to the description 'prayer'—they are separate accidental descriptions. Also, the benefit might be outweighing but not apparent to all, so the lack of consensus does not prove the point.
This example distinguishes between necessary and accidental harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: هل تنخرم مناسبة الوصف للحكم بمفسدة تلزم ذلك الوصف (راجحة) على مصلحة (أو مساوية إياها قيل : لا تنخرم واختاره الإمام الرازي) صاحب «المحصول» من الشافعية (وهو المختار وقيل : نعم تنخرم واختاره ابن الحاجب لنا استحالة الانقلاب) من كونه مناسباً إلى ما ليس مناسباً وعدم التضاد بين إفضائه إلى مصلحة وافضائه إلى مفسدة لتعدد الجهة) في المفروض، فلا استحالة في الاجتماع، واعلم أن الكلام ههنا في مقامين : الأول : أن المفسدة تبطل المناسبة ،وتعدمها، وبه قال قائلو الانخرام، وهذا ضروري البطلان، إذ المفروض كونه مناسباً مشتملاً على مصلحة ومع هذا مشتمل على مفسدة، والواقع لا يبطل . والثاني : أن المفسدة توجب عدم اعتبار الشارع المناسبة معها، وهو مختار صاحب «المحصول»، وجمهور الشافعية، واستدلوا بأن اعتبار مصلحة مع لزوم مفسدة أبعد من الحكيم كل البعد، وما ذكره المصنف لا يبطل هذا بل الوافي به أن مقتضى حكمة الحكيم أن لا يهدر ما هو الواقع، الواقع ههنا مصلحة ،ومفسدة فللحكيم أن يوفي حقهما إذ لا مانع، إذ المانع الذي يتخيل هو التضاد، وهو غير مانع لاختلاف الجهة فافهم فإنه دقيق وبالتأمل حقيق. (ومن ههنا أي من أجل جواز اجتماعهما من جهتين (صح النذر بصوم يوم العيد عند الحنفية فإنه من جهة كونه صوماً منسوباً الله تعالى كاسراً للشهوة فيه مصلحة، فأثر فيه النذر فوجب به، ومن جهة كونه إعراضاً عن ضيافة الله تعالى فيه مفسدة، وهو حرام به وقد مر (وأما عدم اعتبار المفسدة المرجوحة من المصلحة اللازمة للوصف بالاتفاق (فلشدة الاهتمام برعاية المصالح (دونها إذ ليس من شأن الحكيم إهدار خير كثير لشر قليل (واستدل) على المختار (بأن مصلحة الصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة ليست راجحة على مفسدتها (وإلا أجمع على الحل) وإذا لم تكن المصلحة راجحة، فأما مرجوحة أو مساوية، وقد اعتبرت حتى جازت تلك الصلاة (والجواب) ليس المفسدة لازمة لها بل ههنا) (وصفان الصلاة والغصب الأول فيه مصلحة لا غير، والثاني فيه مفسدة لا غير اجتماعهما (اتفاقي فليست من الباب، وأيضاً : يجوز رجحان المصلحة، ولا يلزم منه الاجتماع على الحل بل يجوز عدم انكشاف رحجانها على البعض، فحكموا بالبطلان وإنما يلزم لو كان الكل عالمين بالرجحان فافهم القائلون بالانخرام (قالوا) لو لم ينخرم لبقي المصلحة و لا مصلحة مع معارضة مفسدة مثلها أو راجحة (ضرورة) فلزم الانخرام (أقول : بطلان (الحقيقة أي حقيقة المصلحة (ممنوع) كيف وقد فرض تحققها (وبطلان الاعتبار أي بطلان اعتبار الشارع إياها أيضاً ممنوع بل يجوز اعتبار الشارع الجهتين كما مر ولو سلم بطلان الاعتبار لا يدل على انتفاء المقتضى حتى لا يبقى المناسب مناسباً، بل يجوز أن يكون هو مناسباً وتخلف الاعتبار لمانع المفسدة فتدبر).
Issue: Is the appropriateness of a description for a ruling invalidated by a harm that accompanies that description, which is outweighing or equal to the benefit? It is said: It is not invalidated, and Imam al-Razi, the author of al-Mahsul from the Shafi'is, chose this, and it is the chosen view. And it is said: Yes, it is invalidated, and Ibn al-Hajib chose this. For us [the Hanafis], there is impossibility of transformation from being appropriate to not being appropriate, and there is no contradiction between its leading to benefit and its leading to harm due to multiplicity of aspect in the assumed case, so there is no impossibility in coexistence. Know that the discussion here has two levels: First: The harm nullifies the appropriateness and removes it, and this is what the proponents of invalidation say. This is necessarily invalid, because the description is assumed to be appropriate and containing benefit, and with this it contains harm, and reality does not nullify. Second: The harm necessitates that the Lawgiver does not consider the appropriateness along with it, and this is the choice of the author of al-Mahsul and the majority of Shafi'is. They argued that considering a benefit along with a necessary harm is farthest from the Wise one. What the author mentioned does not nullify this; rather, what suffices is that the requirement of the Wise one's wisdom is not to waste what is real. The reality here is benefit and harm, so the Wise one can give each its due since there is no preventer. The preventer imagined is contradiction, and it is not a preventer due to difference of aspect. Understand, for it is subtle and worthy of contemplation. From this, i.e., from the permissibility of their coexistence from two aspects, the Hanafis consider valid the vow to fast on the day of Eid. For from the aspect of being a fast attributed to Allah, breaking desire, there is benefit, so the vow had effect and it became obligatory; from the aspect of it being a turning away from Allah's hospitality, there is harm, and it is unlawful thereby, as has passed. As for not considering the outweighed harm from the benefit necessary for the description by consensus, it is due to the intense care for observing benefits over them; it is not from the nature of the Wise to waste much good for little evil. They argued for the chosen view that the benefit of prayer in usurped land is not outweighed over its harm, otherwise consensus on permissibility would occur. When the benefit is not outweighing, it is either outweighed or equal, and it has been considered such that that prayer is permissible. The answer: The harm is not necessary for it; rather here there are two descriptions: prayer and usurpation. The first contains only benefit, the second only harm; their coexistence is accidental, so it is not from the subject. Also, the outweighing of benefit is possible, and consensus on permissibility does not follow, rather it is possible that its outweighing is not apparent to some, so they ruled invalidity; it would only follow if all were aware of the outweighing. Understand. Those who say invalidation said: If it were not invalidated, the benefit would remain along with no benefit because of the opposition of a harm similar to it or outweighing necessarily, so invalidation follows. I say: The invalidity of the reality, i.e., the reality of the benefit, is denied; how, while we assumed its existence? And the invalidity of consideration, i.e., the Lawgiver's consideration of it, is also denied; rather, it is permissible for the Lawgiver to consider both aspects as passed. Even if we grant invalidity of consideration, it does not indicate the negation of the effective cause such that the description remains no longer appropriate; rather, it is possible that it remains appropriate and the consideration is omitted due to the preventer of harm. So ponder.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul analysis of whether a description (وصف) that serves as an effective cause (علة) for a ruling loses its appropriateness (مناسبة) if it entails a harm (مفسدة) that is equal to or greater than the benefit (مصلحة) it produces. The Hanafi position, aligned with Imam al-Razi, is that the appropriateness is not invalidated. The key reasoning is that benefit and harm arise from different angles (تعدد الجهة), so they can coexist without contradiction. For example, fasting on Eid day: from one angle it is beneficial (breaking desire, devotion), from another it is harmful (turning away from Allah's hospitality). The Hanafis uphold the validity of a vow to fast on Eid because the benefit is considered from one aspect, even though the act is also prohibited from another aspect. The Shafi'i majority (following Imam al-Razi) argue that the Lawgiver would not consider a benefit accompanied by a necessary harm, but the Hanafi rejoinder is that the harm is not necessarily attached to the description itself—as in the case of prayer in usurped land, where prayer and usurpation are two separate coincidental descriptions. The author concludes that even if the Lawgiver does not consider the benefit, the description may remain appropriate but unapplied due to the preventive harm. This is a subtle point requiring deep reflection.

Key Terms
مناسبة (munasaba): The suitability or appropriateness of a description to produce the ruling's intended benefit.
مصلحة (maslaha): A benefit that the Lawgiver intends to achieve through the ruling.
مفسدة (mafsada): A harm that outweighs or equals the benefit, potentially preventing the Lawgiver's consideration.
تعدد الجهة (ta'addud al-jiha): Multiplicity of aspects; the principle that the same act can be evaluated from different legal perspectives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling on a vow to fast on the day of Eid?
A) It is invalid because the day is prohibited for fasting.
B) It is valid because the benefit (fasting as devotion) and harm (avoiding Allah's hospitality) come from different aspects.
C) It is invalid because the harm outweighs the benefit.
D) It is valid only if the person did not know it was Eid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a description remains appropriate even if it entails a harm from a different aspect; the Lawgiver may still consider the benefit, and the appropriateness is not nullified.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
