Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
15 min
Dispute on Specification of the Cause (Takhsis al-Illa)
Analyzing arguments for and against specification of the legal cause in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on takhsis al-illa and evaluate the objections of the opposing school by analyzing the rebuttals presented in the text.

Core Idea
A cause may be specified by a preventer just as a general text is specified by a particularizer.

This passage presents a foundational dispute in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: can a legal cause (illa) be valid despite a counterexample (naqd)? The Hanafis argue that a preventer (mani') can block the effect of the cause without invalidating its causality, analogous to specifying a general text. The passage systematically presents objections and rebuttals, ultimately showing the dispute may be lexical.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Determine what the dispute is about: whether it is permissible for a legal cause to have a counterexample where the ruling does not apply, i.e., whether the cause can be specified (takhsis).
The cause is the 'illa in qiyas; specification means restricting its application despite a valid counterexample.
2
State the Hanafi position and primary analogy
The Hanafi position, as presented, is that specification is permissible. The key proof is an analogy: just as a general textual command (amm) can be specified without losing its generality, so can a cause be specified by a preventer without losing its causality. Find the phrase: 'لنا تخصيص عموم العلة كتخصيص عموم اللفظ'.
This analogy is the central argument; the cause is treated as a 'general meaning' akin to a general wording.
3
Analyze the first objection and its rebuttal
The opponents argue that specification leads to contradiction because the existence of the cause necessitates the ruling. The Hanafi response is that the preventer acts as an exception; the cause's necessitation is conditional on the absence of a valid preventer.
This rebuttal establishes that no logical contradiction arises when a preventer is present.
4
Examine the objection of tashwib (deeming all mujtahids correct)
Opponents claim that if deferral is allowed, any mujtahid could claim a preventer to justify his error. The Hanafi response requires that a valid preventer must be demonstrated; otherwise, the counterexample invalidates the cause. Thus, not all are deemed correct.
The text also notes other methods of rebuttal besides naqd, so the door to tashwib is closed.
5
Distinguish between the effective cause (mu'aththir) and the complete cause (tammah)
A major objection is that the cause includes conditions and absence of preventer, so a counterexample means the cause is absent. The Hanafi response clarifies that the dispute is about the effective factor alone, not the complete cause. The complete cause includes conditions and absence of preventer; the effective cause is just the motivating description. Thus, deferral from the effective cause does not invalidate it.
This distinction is crucial: the permissors (Hanafis) refer to the effective cause, while the opponents refer to the complete cause. The dispute may be lexical.

The Fourth Principle: Qiyas. That is counted among his merits. He said in al-Tahqiq: Whoever among our mashayikh said that specification of the cause claimed that this is the madhhab of our three imams, end. (And it is said: It is permissible) in the explicit cause only, not the derived cause. (And it is said: It is permissible) in the derived cause only, not the explicit cause. (For us: specification of the generality of the cause is like specification of the generality of the wording) because each of them in its apparent meaning necessitates inclusion, but it is specified in some individuals, so permission must be affirmed. If it is said: the general is a wording and thus accepts specification, contrary to the cause which is a meaning not receptive to it, he said: (And the statement that specification is a quality of wording) so it does not occur in meaning (is a new) convention (that does not repel the meaning) because we say: the cause necessitated the ruling in everything it exists in, but it is deferred due to a preventer that prevents its effect, as in the general that calls for the ruling in everything, and the particularizer prevents in some. So does the prohibition of applying the term specification to the meaning benefit anything? When the preventers of specification argued that if specification and deferral are allowed, contradiction would follow, because existence of the cause requires the ruling to exist in it, and the preventer prevents it, he responded: (And contradiction does not necessarily follow, because the preventer is an exception rationally) so we do not grant that existence of the cause necessitates the ruling when the preventer exists. That would only follow if the effect of the cause remained, which is prevented by the preventer. They also argued: if deferral and specification are allowed, every mujtahid would be deemed correct, because everyone could say about the violation of his cause that the ruling is deferred due to a preventer. He responded: (And correctness of every mujtahid does not follow) as claimed by Imam Fakhr al-Islam, because deferral in the derived cause is only accepted with explanation of a valid preventer. If such preventer appears, the mu'allil is judged mistaken; otherwise, the one who considers the deferral mistaken, so both parties are not deemed correct. Understand that there are many methods of rebuttal besides violation, by which his reasoning is rebutted, so error and correctness become apparent. Also, at most what follows is that we do not know which is the mistaken and which is the correct, and it does not follow that everyone is factually correct. (They said (first): if deferral is allowed, then due to a preventer or absence of a condition, and absence of preventer or presence of condition is part of the cause, because what necessitates the effect is the whole of the effective factor, absence of preventer, and presence of condition, and there is no whole and) the situation is that it is not a part, so with the presence of preventer or absence of condition, the cause ceases, so the ruling ceases by its cessation, so no deferral. (We said: the dispute is only about the description that is the motivator and effective, not about the aggregate of what the known depends on. And condition and absence of preventer have no role in the effect (by agreement). Rather, the effective factor is the description itself, and the ruling may be deferred from it. From here, their statement in the argument is rebutted: if the cause were valid with deferral, the ruling would follow in the case of deferral because existence of the cause necessitates the effect. The rebuttal is that what necessitates the effect is the existence of the complete cause, not just the effective factor, and the dispute is about it. Know that this proof and the two previous proofs indicate that by cause they meant the complete effective factor that combines the conditions of effectiveness, and the proofs are complete about it, because absence of preventer and presence of condition are certainly completers of effectiveness. So when the preventer exists or the condition is absent, the complete effective factor ceases, so the ruling ceases by it, so no deferral. Also, if the ruling were deferred from the complete effective factor, the ruling would exist in the case of deferral, and contradiction would follow because it necessitates the ruling. Also, it would necessitate the correctness of non-existence of the ruling because it was not a harm to the completeness of the cause; everyone could claim causality for every description, and the violation would not harm at all. So the more plausible is that the dispute is lexical. Whoever allows deferral allows it from the effective factor that does not gather the conditions of effect, and whoever prevents it prevents it from the complete effective factor. The author of al-Kashf said: the disagreement on the issue of specification of the cause goes back to wording, because the cause in a non-deferral place is valid according to both parties, and in the deferral place the ruling is non-existent, except that non-existence according to the preventer is attributed to non-existence, and according to the permissor to the preventer. The author said: and the truth is that it is meaningful, its fruit appears in the response to violation. According to the permissor, it is permissible by showing the preventer, not without it. And in the issue of corruption of appropriateness by the presence of an inseparable preponderant or equal harmful factor, according to the permissor there is no corruption, but rather deferral due to a preventer, and according to the preventer it is corrupted. End. This is not as it should be: rather, the non-existence of the ruling is certainly necessary for the preventer; the permissor attributes it to it, and the preventer attributes it to the non-existence of the cause because of the inclusion of absence of preventer in it. As for the corruption of appropriateness by a preponderant or equal harmful factor, this statement has not been established from the preventer such that it would be a fruit. Understand. (And) they said (secondly: conflict between the proof of consideration, i.e., consideration of the cause which is a path of its paths, and the proof of disregarding, which is the proof of its violation. And conflict necessitates dropping, so no consideration) so no causality. (We said: the existence of the proof of disregarding is denied. Rather, violation is not proof of disregarding except without a preventer, and the discussion is when it exists, so no dropping. And if you reflect, you know that the proof is complete if the complete cause is intended, for the deferral of the ruling is proof of the incompleteness of the cause. (And) they said (thirdly: the legal cause is like the rational cause) in necessitating the ruling, (and there is no specification in it) so there is no specification in the legal one. (And it was answered that rational causes are causes by essence, and what is by essence is inseparable) so their causality and effect are inseparable, so the effect is inseparable from them. (And these) i.e., the legal causes (are causes by the positing of the Lawgiver, so they may not necessitate their effect, so they differ. (This is) in al-Mukhtasar. I say this answer is unsatisfactory because the Lawgiver made them necessitators of the ruling, and His making is a fact, so they and the rational causes are equal in necessitation; it does not defer without a preventer, as assumed. And from there, the preventer is assumed in the explicit cause by agreement, so the difference between them and the rational cause is pointless. Rather, the truth in the answer is that the rational effective factor, which is the agent cause, is like the legal effective factor; deferral due to a preventer is permissible in it, even if deferral is not permissible in the complete. Do you not see that wet wood does not burn from the fire that burns because wetness prevents burning? And the complete legal cause does not permit deferral, and the permissor's discussion is not about it, but about the effective factor. We are confident that you have realized that analogizing the legal cause to the rational cause is a clear indication that the preventer intended the complete cause, so it is established and not shaken. And Allah knows best the intent of His noble servants. The preventers in the derived cause (said: if the derived cause were valid with this deferral, there must be a preventer, otherwise if there is no preventer, no causality. And the preventer is only after the cause, otherwise if there is no existence of cause with the existence of preventer, then the non-existence of ruling is due to non-existence of cause, not to the preventer. So the cause depends on the preventer, and it depends on the cause, so circularity. And it was answered that it is circularity of concomitance, i.e., necessary connection between causality and preventiveness, and the dependence of one on the other is denied. And it is repelled by saying that what is meant is not the dependence of each in reality on the other, but the knowledge of each depends on knowledge of the other. It is explained that what is meant is that preventiveness is not known except after causality, i.e., knowledge of it; otherwise the absence of the prerequisite is possible, so preventiveness is not known. And causality is not known at the time of deferral except after deferral.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul al-fiqh dispute on the permissibility of specifying a legal cause (takhsis al-illa) when a counterexample (naqd) occurs in qiyas. The author, as a Hanafi commentator, supports the view that specification is allowed, analogizing it to the specification of a general textual command (takhsis al-amm). The text first presents three positions: (1) specification is allowed only in the explicit cause (manqusa), (2) only in the derived cause (mustanbita), (3) the preferred Hanafi view that it is allowed in both. The key argument is that just as a general text (amm) may be specified by a particularizing evidence without rendering the general false, similarly a cause (illa) may fail to produce its ruling in some cases due to a preventer (mani') without invalidating its causality. The author rebuts objections: (a) the objection that specification leads to contradiction because the cause necessitates the ruling is answered by noting that the presence of a preventer is an exception; (b) the objection that it leads to tashwib (deeming every mujtahid correct) is answered by requiring a valid preventer for acceptance; (c) the objection that the cause includes conditions and absence of preventer is refuted by distinguishing between the effective cause (mu'aththir) and the complete cause (tammah); (d) the objection that legal causes are like rational causes which cannot be specified is rejected by noting that both may have deferrals due to preventers (e.g., wet wood not burning despite fire). The passage concludes that the dispute may be lexical, as permissors refer to the incomplete effective factor and preventers to the complete cause. A key nuance is the Hanafi position that the cause (illa) is the motivator (ba'ith) that influences the ruling, not the entire set of conditions.

Key Terms
العلة (al-illa): The legal cause or effective factor that necessitates a ruling in qiyas; the description that motivates the ruling.
التخصيص (al-takhsis): Specification or restriction of a general ruling or cause to some cases, excluding others due to a preventer.
النقض (al-naqd): A counterexample where the cause exists but the ruling does not, used as an objection to the cause's validity.
المانع (al-mani'): A preventer that blocks the effect of the cause although the cause is present.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the primary analogy used to justify the permissibility of specification of the cause?
A) Analogy between legal cause and rational cause
B) Analogy between specification of cause and specification of general texts
C) Analogy between cause and condition
D) Analogy between explicit and derived causes

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits specification of a cause by a preventer, preserving the validity of qiyas despite apparent counterexamples.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Resolving the Circularity between Mani' and 'Illah
Analyzing the circularity argument and its resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the circularity problem between mani' and 'illah, and explain how the Hanafi school resolves it by distinguishing between actuality (bi'l-fi'l) and potentiality (bi'l-quwwah).

Core Idea
A potential counter (bi'l-quwwah) breaks the circle: the 'illah depends only on the possibility of a mani', not its actuality.

In this lesson, we examine a key debate in Hanafi usul: does the absence of a ruling (takhalluf) without a known preventive factor (mani') destroy the probability (zann) that the 'illah is effective? Some argue this leads to a vicious circle. The Hanafi response, as presented in this passage, elegantly distinguishes between the actual mani' and the potential mani' to preserve the integrity of qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the circularity
Understand the dependency between zann of the 'illah and knowledge of the mani'. The absence of a ruling without a known mani' causes hesitation about the 'illah's efficacy.
The initial claim is that this leads to a vicious circle.
2
Recognize the initial objection
An opponent argues that takhalluf without a known mani' makes the 'illah doubtful, leading to circularity because zann of 'illah depends on the mani' and vice versa.
This is the opposing position.
3
Follow the proposed solution
The Hanafi solution distinguishes between the actual existence of a mani' (bi'l-fi'l) and its mere possibility (bi'l-quwwah). What depends on the 'illah is the actual mani', but the 'illah itself depends only on the potential mani'.
This breaks the circular dependency.
4
Apply to an example
Consider the case of two poor people: one is given charity, the other (a sinful person) is denied. The 'illah of poverty is zanni, but the denial (takhalluf) is due to the potential mani' of sinfulness. The possibility of sin as a mani' preserves the zann of the 'illah.
This example illustrates the solution.
5
Appreciate the result
By assuming a potential mani', the circle is broken, and qiyas remains valid even when a ruling is absent. The 'illah remains zanni based on its paths, independent of actual knowledge of the mani'.
This preserves the utility of derived causes.

الأصل الرابع/ القياس العلم بالمانعية) فإنّ التخلف من غير علم المانع يوجب التردد في اقتضاء المؤثر، فحينئذ لزم الدور (وقد يجاب بأن ظن العلية يحصل (بمسالكها) من غير توقف على العلم بالمانع واستمراره موقوف لتسليم التخصيص ( فالتقدير) للمانع (هو الحق) في الجواب (فتدبر).
The fourth principle: Qiyas: Knowledge of the preventive factor (mani'iyyah). For the absence (of the ruling) without knowledge of the preventive factor causes hesitation in the efficacy of the effect, so then there is circularity. And it may be answered that zann of causality is obtained through its paths without depending on knowledge of the preventive factor, and its continuity depends on the preventive factor, i.e., knowledge of it at the time of absence, for absence is suspicious except with the preventive factor. And the preventive factor depends on the original zann of causality, not on its continuity, so there is no circularity. I say: The preventive factor, i.e., zann of it in the place of absence, depends on zann of it (causality) there, otherwise it is possible that the ruling is absent due to absence of the cause. And zann of it (causality) there depends on the preventive factor, i.e., on zann of it (the preventive factor) there, because absence without a preventive factor is evidence of the absence of the cause, and the paths do not benefit zann of causality in the place where the ruling is absent, even if they benefit elsewhere, except with the existence of the preventive factor. So it results in circularity. And it is also problematic if knowledge of the absence coincides with zann of the cause, as when two poor people ask him and he gives to one and prevents the sinful one: then causality of poverty is not obtained except after zann that sinfulness is a preventive factor, and the paths alone are insufficient. And the correct answer is that what depends on causality and knowledge of it is the preventive factor in actuality (bi'l-fi'l) and zann of it, and what it depends on is the cause and zann of it, which is the preventive factor in potentiality (bi'l-quwwah) and zann of it. And it is doubt whether a thing, when it accompanies a motivator and prevents its effect, this thing exists or not. This is the explicit preventive factors. They said: The evidence of the derived cause from its paths necessitates zann of it, and the absence is doubtful due to the possibility of a preventive factor in the place of absence, so it is a cause. And the possibility of its absence there, so it is not a cause. So there is no conflict between its evidence and the evidence of its absence, which is the absence, due to the preponderance of the first. And it was answered: Doubt about one of the two opposites necessitates the other because it is equal possibility of both sides. So your statement: 'causality is zanni and its absence is doubtful' is contradictory; rather, causality also becomes doubtful. If you say: then what is the meaning of their statement: 'zann is not removed by doubt'? He said: 'As for the statement of the jurists that zann is not removed by doubt, its meaning is that the ruling of the stronger, which is established, is not removed by the weaker that arises, legally, i.e., the Law has obligated acting upon the stronger, even if the weaker opposing arises.' And such is not possible here, because the discussion here is about zann itself: does it occur at the time of absence or not? I say: It can be established like this: The absence in itself, without considering the occurrence of something, is doubtful. So when it is combined with evidence of causality and the possibility of a preventive factor, causality becomes zanni with strong zann, because the possibility of the absence of efficacy is eliminated by its evidence, and the doubtful becomes zanni by the preponderant necessarily. So the correct answer is that individually, both the evidence of the derived cause and the absence necessitate zann for both causality and its non-existence. And when they come together, doubt occurs in both sides due to conflict between them. So we do not accept your statement that the absence is doubtful; rather, it benefits the non-existence of causality. And there is something in it: The possibility of the existence of the preventive factor and its absence both stand equally. So the absence itself is doubtful, so there is no room for denial, unless one claims that the possibility of the absence of the preventive factor is remote due to the frequency of the absence of the ruling due to the absence of the cause, and the original principle is zann of non-causality. So understand. As for the explicit cause, it does not accept rebuttal, and the absence of the ruling from it necessitates the invalidity of the general text that benefits the binding of the ruling to it. For specification of the cause is like saying: Everything in which the cause exists, the ruling exists, unlike the derived cause, for its evidence is conjunction, i.e., conjunction of the ruling with the absence of the preventive factor, so absence is allowed due to its possibility. And it was answered in Al-Mukhtasar: If the text is definitive, then non-acceptance of specification is agreed upon, and there is no dispute. Otherwise, it accepts specification, and the preventive factor is assumed. And this is not from the invalidity of the text, but rather it is figurative due to its evidence. I say: The rebuttal, i.e., rebuttal of the cause, is assumed and hypothetical, and the discussion about it, even if it is an impossible assumption because it is definitive, then there is no meaning to accepting specification. So the assumption of the preventive factor is the truth in the answer. So ponder.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion about the relationship between the 'illah (effective cause) and the mani' (preventive factor) in qiyas. The author begins by stating that if takhalluf (absence of the ruling) occurs without knowledge of a mani', it causes hesitation about the efficacy of the 'illah, leading to a circular argument. He then presents a common response: zann of 'illah is obtained through its paths (masalik) without needing knowledge of the mani', and the continuation of that zann depends on the mani' at the time of takhalluf. But the mani' itself depends on the original zann of 'illah, not its continuation, so there is no circularity. However, the author (the commentator) disagrees and points out that zann of the mani' in the place of takhalluf depends on zann of the 'illah there, because without a mani', the absence indicates the absence of the cause. Conversely, zann of the 'illah there depends on zann of the mani', because the paths of 'illah do not establish zann in a case of takhalluf unless a mani' is presumed. Thus circularity remains. He then presents an example: two poor people, one given charity, the other denied because he is sinful. The 'illah (poverty) is zanni, but takhalluf (denial) requires assuming sinfulness as a mani'. Without that assumption, the 'illah is not established. The correct answer, according to the author, is to distinguish between the mani' in actuality (bi'l-fi'l) and in potentiality (bi'l-quwwah). What depends on the 'illah is the actual mani' (its actual existence and zann), while what the 'illah depends on is the potential mani' (its possibility). This resolves the circularity. The passage then contrasts two types of 'illah: mansusah (explicitly stated) and mustanbatah (derived). The explicit cause does not accept rebuttal (naqd) because it is based on a general text that universally links cause to ruling. The derived cause, however, relies on conjunction (iqitiran) of ruling with absence of mani', so takhalluf is possible due to a potential mani'. The author references Al-Mukhtasar's view: if the text is definitive, specification is impossible; otherwise, specification is allowed by assuming a mani'. The author concludes that the correct response is to assume the mani' when needed, not to invalidate the text.

Key Terms
مانع (mani'): A preventive factor that blocks the effect of the 'illah, allowing takhalluf of the ruling.
علة ('illah): The effective cause that necessitates a ruling. In this passage, it is the subject of zann and may be explicit or derived.
ظن (zann): Strong probability, not certainty. The passage discusses whether zann of 'illah survives takhalluf.
بالفعل وبالقوة (bi'l-fi'l wa bi'l-quwwah): Distinction between actual existence (bi'l-fi'l) and potential existence (bi'l-quwwah). Applied to the mani' to resolve circularity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, how is the circularity (dawr) between mani' and 'illah resolved?
A) By rejecting the concept of mani' altogether
B) By distinguishing between mani' bi'l-fi'l and mani' bi'l-quwwah
C) By requiring explicit text for both mani' and 'illah
D) By asserting that zann is always stronger than shakk

Key Takeaway
Zann of 'illah can survive takhalluf when a potential mani' (bi'l-quwwah) is assumed, breaking the circular dependency.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
7 min
Explicit vs. Derived 'Illah: The Role of Mani' in Specification
Distinguishing the treatment of explicit and derived 'illahs regarding takhalluf and specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between explicit (mansusah) and derived (mustanbatah) 'illah, and explain why an explicit 'illah based on a definitive text cannot be subject to specification while a derived 'illah can accommodate a potential mani'.

Core Idea
An explicit 'illah from a definitive text is binding and resistant to specification; a derived 'illah is open to specification through a presumed mani'.

In the previous lesson, we saw how a potential mani' preserves the zann of a derived 'illah. Now we turn to the contrast between explicit 'illahs, which are textually stated, and derived 'illahs, which are inferred. The Hanafi school holds that an explicit 'illah from a definitive text (qat'i) is not open to specification (takhsis), whereas a non-definitive explicit 'illah can be specified by assuming a mani'. This lesson clarifies that nuanced position.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two types of 'illah
Identify the explicit 'illah (mansusah) which is clearly stated in a text, and the derived 'illah (mustanbatah) which is inferred through masalik.
The passage discusses both and their differing treatments.
2
Understand the ruling for explicit 'illah
An explicit 'illah from a definitive text (qat'i) does not accept rebuttal (naqd) because the text universally links cause and ruling.
Specification would contradict the universal wording.
3
Analyze the exception for non-definitive texts
If the text is not definitive (zanni), then specification is allowed by assuming a mani'. This does not invalidate the text but interprets it figuratively.
This compromises between textual authority and practical exceptions.
4
Apply to derived 'illah
Derived 'illahs always allow for a potential mani' because their evidence is based on conjunction, not universal linkage.
Thus takhalluf can always be explained by a potential mani'.
5
Conclude the implications
The assumption of a mani' is a valid interpretive tool; it does not invalidate the text but rather specifies it.
This preserves the utility of both explicit and derived causes.

The fourth principle: Qiyas: Knowledge of the preventive factor (mani'iyyah). For the absence (of the ruling) without knowledge of the preventive factor causes hesitation in the efficacy of the effect, so then there is circularity. And it may be answered that zann of causality is obtained through its paths without depending on knowledge of the preventive factor, and its continuity depends on the preventive factor, i.e., knowledge of it at the time of absence, for absence is suspicious except with the preventive factor. And the preventive factor depends on the original zann of causality, not on its continuity, so there is no circularity. I say: The preventive factor, i.e., zann of it in the place of absence, depends on zann of it (causality) there, otherwise it is possible that the ruling is absent due to absence of the cause. And zann of it (causality) there depends on the preventive factor, i.e., on zann of it (the preventive factor) there, because absence without a preventive factor is evidence of the absence of the cause, and the paths do not benefit zann of causality in the place where the ruling is absent, even if they benefit elsewhere, except with the existence of the preventive factor. And it is doubt whether a thing, when it accompanies a motivator and prevents its effect, this thing exists or not. This is the explicit preventive factors. They said: The evidence of the derived cause from its paths necessitates zann of it, and the absence is doubtful due to the possibility of a preventive factor in the place of absence, so it is a cause. And the possibility of its absence there, so it is not a cause. So there is no conflict between its evidence and the evidence of its absence, which is the absence, due to the preponderance of the first. And it was answered: Doubt about one of the two opposites necessitates the other because it is equal possibility of both sides. So your statement: 'causality is zanni and its absence is doubtful' is contradictory; rather, causality also becomes doubtful. If you say: then what is the meaning of their statement: 'zann is not removed by doubt'? He said: 'As for the statement of the jurists that zann is not removed by doubt, its meaning is that the ruling of the stronger, which is established, is not removed by the weaker that arises, legally, i.e., the Law has obligated acting upon the stronger, even if the weaker opposing arises.' And such is not possible here, because the discussion here is about zann itself: does it occur at the time of absence or not? I say: It can be established like this: The absence in itself, without considering the occurrence of something, is doubtful. So when it is combined with evidence of causality and the possibility of a preventive factor, causality becomes zanni with strong zann, because the possibility of the absence of efficacy is eliminated by its evidence, and the doubtful becomes zanni by the preponderant necessarily. So the correct answer is that individually, both the evidence of the derived cause and the absence necessitate zann for both causality and its non-existence. And when they come together, doubt occurs in both sides due to conflict between them. So we do not accept your statement that the absence is doubtful; rather, it benefits the non-existence of causality. And there is something in it: The possibility of the existence of the preventive factor and its absence both stand equally. So the absence itself is doubtful, so there is no room for denial, unless one claims that the possibility of the absence of the preventive factor is remote due to the frequency of the absence of the ruling due to the absence of the cause, and the original principle is zann of non-causality. So understand. As for the explicit cause, it does not accept rebuttal, and the absence of the ruling from it necessitates the invalidity of the general text that benefits the binding of the ruling to it. For specification of the cause is like saying: Everything in which the cause exists, the ruling exists, unlike the derived cause, for its evidence is conjunction, i.e., conjunction of the ruling with the absence of the preventive factor, so absence is allowed due to its possibility. And it was answered in Al-Mukhtasar: If the text is definitive, then non-acceptance of specification is agreed upon, and there is no dispute. Otherwise, it accepts specification, and the preventive factor is assumed. And this is not from the invalidity of the text, but rather it is figurative due to its evidence. I say: The rebuttal, i.e., rebuttal of the cause, is assumed and hypothetical, and the discussion about it, even if it is an impossible assumption because it is definitive, then there is no meaning to accepting specification. So the assumption of the preventive factor is the truth in the answer. So ponder.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion about the relationship between the 'illah (effective cause) and the mani' (preventive factor) in qiyas. The author begins by stating that if takhalluf (absence of the ruling) occurs without knowledge of a mani', it causes hesitation about the efficacy of the 'illah, leading to a circular argument. He then presents a common response: zann of 'illah is obtained through its paths (masalik) without needing knowledge of the mani', and the continuation of that zann depends on the mani' at the time of takhalluf. But the mani' itself depends on the original zann of 'illah, not its continuation, so there is no circularity. However, the author (the commentator) disagrees and points out that zann of the mani' in the place of takhalluf depends on zann of the 'illah there, because without a mani', the absence indicates the absence of the cause. Conversely, zann of the 'illah there depends on zann of the mani', because the paths of 'illah do not establish zann in a case of takhalluf unless a mani' is presumed. Thus circularity remains. He then presents an example: two poor people, one given charity, the other denied because he is sinful. The 'illah (poverty) is zanni, but takhalluf (denial) requires assuming sinfulness as a mani'. Without that assumption, the 'illah is not established. The correct answer, according to the author, is to distinguish between the mani' in actuality (bi'l-fi'l) and in potentiality (bi'l-quwwah). What depends on the 'illah is the actual mani' (its actual existence and zann), while what the 'illah depends on is the potential mani' (its possibility). This resolves the circularity. The passage then contrasts two types of 'illah: mansusah (explicitly stated) and mustanbatah (derived). The explicit cause does not accept rebuttal (naqd) because it is based on a general text that universally links cause to ruling. The derived cause, however, relies on conjunction (iqitiran) of ruling with absence of mani', so takhalluf is possible due to a potential mani'. The author references Al-Mukhtasar's view: if the text is definitive, specification is impossible; otherwise, specification is allowed by assuming a mani'. The author concludes that the correct response is to assume the mani' when needed, not to invalidate the text.

Key Terms
علة منصوصة ('illah mansusah): An explicit cause clearly stated in a legal text.
علة مستنبطة ('illah mustanbatah): A derived cause inferred through the paths of qiyas.
نقض (naqd): Rebutting an 'illah by showing a case where the cause exists but the ruling is absent.
تخصيص (takhsis): Specification of a general text, narrowing its application.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, under what condition can an explicit 'illah be specified?
A) Never
B) Only if the text is definitive
C) Only if the text is non-definitive
D) Always

Key Takeaway
An explicit 'illah from a definitive text is immune to specification; a non-definitive explicit 'illah can be specified by assuming a mani'.

Five Types of Mani' and the Hanafi View on Kasr
Enumerating five types of preventive factors in 'illah and discussing kasr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the five types of preventive factors (mawani') affecting the 'illah and explain why kasr does not invalidate the 'illah according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The 'illah is the regulated mazannah, not the unregulated hikmah; kasr does not break it.

This passage from a Hanafi usul text classifies five distinct ways a factor can prevent the legal cause ('illah) from producing its ruling. Understanding these categories is essential for correctly applying qiyas. The text then tackles the important concept of 'kasr'—when the wisdom (hikmah) behind the ruling is absent while the mazannah is present—and defends the majority Hanafi view that such kasr does not invalidate the 'illah. Mastering this distinction is key to appreciating the Hanafi approach to legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the Five Types
Learn the five categories of mani' by their effect on the 'illah: (1) prevents formation, (2) prevents completion/effect, (3) prevents beginning of ruling, (4) prevents completion of ruling, (5) prevents binding. Associate each with its example from the text.
Use the examples as anchors for recall: freedom, selling others' property, option of stipulation, option of inspection, option of defect.
2
Understand the Mechanism of Each Type
For each mani', ask: Does the 'illah still exist? Is the ruling possible? The answer varies by type. For example, in type 1 (in'iqad), the 'illah itself fails; in type 3 (ibtida'), the 'illah is effective but its effect is delayed.
This step builds a functional understanding, not just rote memorization.
3
Define Kasr and State the Hanafi Position
Kasr is when the hikmah is absent but the 'illah (mazannah) is present. The Hanafi school holds that kasr does not invalidate the 'illah because the 'illah is the regulated mazannah, not the unregulated hikmah.
Contrast with the minority view that the 'illah is subordinate to the hikmah.
4
Reconstruct the Hanafi Argument with the Virginity Example
Trace the reasoning: Virginity (bikarah) is the 'illah for a woman's silence being consent. The hikmah is shyness. But shyness is unregulated, so the legislator used virginity as a mazannah. Even if a non-virgin is shyer, her silence is not consent because the 'illah (virginity) is absent. This shows kasr does not break the 'illah.
This example concretely demonstrates the principle.

وامتنع اعتبار إطلاقها في تعليق الحكم به في نظر الشارع وتعذر تعيين القدر الصالح) للاعتبار بحيث ينضبط عند المكلف ضبطت بما هو أمارة (له ومظنة تيسيراً على المكلف، فتكون هذه المظنة هي المعتبرة شرعاً في لشرع حكم لا بد من تشريع) حكم أليق) بكل من الأقدار ( كالقطع) أي وجوبه (بالقطع) العمد العدوان فإنه ضابط
Branch: The preventive factors as mentioned in our books are five: First: What prevents the formation of the cause, like selling a free person, for freedom prevents it from being a sale. Second: What prevents its completion and actual effect in necessitating the ruling, like selling the property of another, for although it is suitable for necessitating the ruling, it is not complete in it, for it is not completed except by ratification because it is his property. Third: What prevents the beginning of the ruling, like the option of stipulation for the seller prevents ownership for the buyer, although it is effective in reality, but its effect depends on the absence of the option, so after it is removed, ownership is established from the origin. Fourth: What prevents its completion, i.e., completion of the ruling even if its beginning is established, like the option of inspection: it does not prevent ownership itself, but ownership is not completed with possession along with it, rather he is allowed to return it without judgment or consent, and this is a sign of incompletion of ownership. Fifth: What prevents its binding effect, i.e., binding of the ruling, like the option of defect: it prevents only the binding of ownership, the buyer cannot rescind after possession except by judgment or mutual consent. If it were binding, it would not be rescinded forcibly by judgment, and if ownership were not completed, he would not need both for rescission. So understand. As for kasr, it is the wisdom (hikmah) of the absence of the ruling from (a case) other than the cause, which is the mazannah, like the absence of the concession of travel from hard labor in residence. According to some, kasr is applied to the broken rebuttal that will come. The chosen view is that it does not invalidate the causality, and this is the view of the majority, contrary to some. For us, the cause is the mazannah, not the hikmah, and it is safe from rebuttal. As for the first premise: because hikmah, when its consideration is necessary in linking the ruling, and it is impossible to consider it absolutely in attaching the ruling to it in the view of the Lawgiver, and it is difficult to determine the suitable amount that can be regulated by the obligated person, it is regulated by what is a sign of it and a mazannah, to facilitate for the obligated person. So this mazannah is considered legally in linking the ruling, so it is the cause, and the hikmah is disregarded. And what is in Al-Minhaj: knowledge of the description (made cause) containing it (the suitable amount) without knowledge of it (this amount) is impossible, so it must be known, then it is the cause. I say this is repelled: because the difficulty of determining it in actuality, such that no hesitation remains, does not contradict regulating it by estimation based on what is generally entailed by it. Ponder. The minority said: The description made a cause is subordinate to the hikmah, for it is only considered for its sake, so it is the true cause. So the rebuttal that occurs on it occurs on the cause and invalidates the causality. We say: The description, even if it is considered for the sake of the hikmah, does not necessarily mean it is the cause. Rather, it has no consideration unless it is regulated. And then the cause is the mazannah. Do you not see that virginity is the cause for being content with silence in marriage? The hikmah is shyness due to its prevalence in her, and the non-virgin even if she were more shy, her silence is not considered by consensus because the degrees of shyness are not regulated in themselves; rather, they are regulated by virginity. Yes: if they had different degrees and for each degree there is a regulating description suitable for legislating a ruling, it would be necessary to legislate a more appropriate ruling for each degree, like amputation, i.e., its obligation, for amputation (as a punishment for intentional transgression) is a regulator.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of five types of mawani' (preventive factors) that affect the 'illah in different ways. The first, mani' of in'iqad (formation), prevents the cause from being valid at all, like selling a free person: freedom makes the sale void ab initio. The second, mani' of tamam (completion) and actual effect, allows the cause to exist but requires something extra for the ruling to take effect, like selling someone else's property: the sale is valid but requires the owner's ratification. The third, mani' of ibtida' (beginning) of the ruling, prevents the ruling from starting but does not negate the cause's potential, like the seller's option of stipulation: it delays the transfer of ownership until the option period ends. The fourth, mani' of tamam (completion) of the ruling after its beginning, like the buyer's option of inspection: ownership begins but is not completed until possession, and the buyer can return the item without legal proceedings. The fifth, mani' of luzum (binding effect), prevents the ruling from becoming binding, like the option of defect: the buyer can rescind but only through court or mutual consent. The passage then discusses 'kasr', which is when the hikmah (wisdom) of the ruling is absent even though the 'illah (mazannah) is present. For example, the concession of travel (e.g., shortening prayer) is not given for hard labor at home, even though both involve hardship. The majority view is that kasr does not invalidate the 'illah because the 'illah is the mazannah (a reliable indicator) not the hikmah itself. The hikmah is not directly regulable, so the Lawgiver uses a mazannah as the 'illah for practical ease. The minority argues that the 'illah is subordinate to the hikmah, so if the hikmah is absent, the 'illah fails. The Hanafi response is that the 'illah is only valid if it is regulated (mundabit), and the mazannah is regulated even if the hikmah is not. The example of virginity in marriage illustrates this: the 'illah for a virgin's silence being consent is her virginity (a regulated attribute), not the hikmah of shyness (which is unregulated). If shyness were regulated, a different 'illah would be used, but it isn't.

Key Terms
كسر (kasr): The absence of the hikmah (wisdom) behind the ruling while the 'illah (mazannah) is present; it does not invalidate the 'illah according to the majority.
حكمة (hikmah): The underlying wisdom or purpose of the ruling, which is not directly regulable; the 'illah is the regulated indicator of this wisdom.
مظنة (mazannah): A reliable indicator that typically aligns with the hikmah; the 'illah is actually the mazannah, not the hikmah itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of mani' prevents the completion (tamam) of the ruling, allowing the ruling to begin but not be fully effective?
A) Mani' of formation (in'iqad)
B) Mani' of completion (tamam) of the ruling
C) Mani' of binding (luzum)
D) Mani' of beginning (ibtida')

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines the 'illah as the regulated mazannah, not the unregulated hikmah, so kasr (absence of wisdom) does not invalidate the cause.

The Five Preventers of the 'Illah
Enumerating five types of mawani' that block the 'illah at various stages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five types of preventers (mawaniʿ) of the ʿillah and classify a given example to the correct type.

Core Idea
A preventer does not destroy the 'illah — it merely blocks its operation at a specific stage.

In Hanafi usul, the 'illah (effective cause) in qiyas is not always unimpeded. This passage enumerates five distinct types of mawani' (preventers) that block the 'illah at various junctures: from its very inception to the bindingness of the resulting ruling. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying qiyas and for evaluating whether a ruling is fully effective, temporarily suspended, or never takes effect.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the fivefold classification
Memorize that there are five distinct preventers, each operating at a different stage of the 'illah's career: inception, completion/effect, beginning of the ruling, completion of the ruling, and bindingness of the ruling.
These are not mutually exclusive in application but conceptually distinct.
2
Match each preventer to its example
For each type, note the concrete example provided: free person (inception), others' property (completion/effect), seller's option (beginning of ruling), option of sighting (completion of ruling), option of defect (bindingness).
The examples come from sales and options (khiyarat) but the principle generalises.
3
Understand the stage that is blocked
For each example, identify which stage the preventer blocks: does it prevent the 'illah from forming, from being fully effective, the ruling from starting, the ruling from being complete, or the ruling from being binding?
The 'illah itself remains valid; only its operation is suspended at a specific point.
4
Apply to a new example
Test your understanding by taking a different scenario (e.g., a gift with a condition) and determine which type of preventer is at work.
Justify your choice by referring to the stage that is blocked.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرع
(الموانع كما ذكر في كتبنا خمسة) الأول (ما يمنع انعقاد العلة كبيع الحر) فإن الحرية مانعة عن كونه بيعاً (و) ثانيها (ما يمنع تمامها وتأثيرها بالفعل في إيجاب الحكم (كبيع عبد الغير) فإنه وإن كان صالحاً لإيجاب الحكم لكنه غير تام فيه فإنه لا يتم إلا بالإجازة) لكونه ملكاً له (و) ثالثها (ما يمنع ابتداء الحكم، كخيار الشرط للبائع يمنع الملك للمشتري) مع كونه مؤثراً حقيقة، لكن تأثيره متوقف على انتفاء الخيار ولذا بعد ارتفاعه يثبت الملك من الأصل (و) رابعها (ما يمنع تمامه) أي تمام الحكم وإن ثبت ابتداؤه (كخيار الرؤية لا الملك) يمنع نفسه لكن لا يتم الملك بالقبض معه، بل يجوز له الرد بلا قضاء ولا رضا) وهذا آية عدم تمام الملك (و) خامسها (ما يمنع لزومه أي لزوم الحكم كخيار العيب) المانع من لزوم الملك فقط (لا يتمكن المشتري من الفسخ بعد القبض إلا بقضاء أو تراض) ولو لزم لما انفسخ جبراً بالقضاء ولو لم يتم الملك لم يحتج في الفسخ إليهما فافهم
A branch: The preventers, as mentioned in our books, are five. First: what prevents the inception of the 'illah, like selling a free person; for freedom prevents it from being a sale. Second: what prevents its completion and its actual effect in necessitating the ruling, like selling the property of another; for although it is suitable for necessitating the ruling, it is not complete in it, as it only becomes complete with ratification, because it is owned by him. Third: what prevents the beginning of the ruling, like the option of condition for the seller prevents ownership for the buyer, while it is effective in reality, but its effect depends on the absence of the option, so after it is removed, ownership is established from the origin. Fourth: what prevents its completion, i.e., completion of the ruling even if its beginning is established, like the option of sighting; it does not prevent ownership itself, but ownership is not completed with possession along with it; rather, he is allowed to return without judgment or consent, and this is a sign of incomplete ownership. Fifth: what prevents its bindingness, i.e., bindingness of the ruling, like the option of defect; it only prevents bindingness of ownership; the buyer cannot rescind after possession except by judgment or mutual consent. If it were binding, it would not be nullified forcibly by judgment, and if ownership were not complete, he would not need both for rescission. So understand.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the types of preventers (mawani') that affect the 'illah in qiyas. The author lists five types based on the stage at which the prevention occurs. The first prevents the 'illah from even being conceived (e.g., selling a free person is not a valid sale because freedom prevents the contract from being considered a sale). The second prevents the 'illah from being fully effective (e.g., selling another's property is a valid contract but not fully effective until the owner ratifies). The third prevents the ruling from taking effect at its inception (e.g., the seller's option to rescind prevents immediate ownership transfer). The fourth prevents the ruling from being complete even though it has begun (e.g., the buyer's option of sighting allows return without court order, showing incomplete ownership). The fifth prevents the ruling from being binding (e.g., the option of defect requires court or mutual consent for rescission). Each type shows how the 'illah may be present but its effect is blocked at different junctures, without invalidating the 'illah itself.

Key Terms
المانع (al-māniʿ): A preventer that blocks the operation of the 'illah at various stages, but does not invalidate the 'illah itself.
انعقاد العلة (inʿiqād al-ʿillah): The inception or formation of the 'illah as a valid cause for the ruling.
تمام العلة (tamām al-ʿillah): The completeness of the 'illah in terms of its effectiveness and actual impact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Prevents inception of the 'illah
B) Prevents completion of the 'illah
C) Prevents beginning of the ruling
D) Prevents completion of the ruling

Key Takeaway
The five preventers of the 'illah show that a valid 'illah can be blocked at different stages: inception, effectiveness, initiation, completion, or bindingness of the ruling.

Kasr and the Sign-Based 'Illah
Analyzing the defect of kasr in 'illah and its Hanafi refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the concept of kasr and explain why the Hanafi school holds that it does not invalidate the 'illah, and apply this to an example.

Core Idea
Kasr does not break the ‘illah — the ‘illah is a reliable sign, not the wisdom itself.

Kasr is a potential defect in analogical reasoning (qiyas) where the wisdom (hikmah) intended by the ruling is absent even though the 'illah (legal cause) is present. The Hanafi school defends the validity of the 'illah in such cases by arguing that the 'illah is not the wisdom itself but a reliable sign (mazinnah) for it. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows why occasional absence of wisdom does not invalidate the 'illah.

Guided Path
1
Define kasr
Understand the term: kasr is when the wisdom (hikmah) lags behind the 'illah, i.e., the 'illah is present but the intended benefit or rationale is absent in a particular instance.
Example: travel concession applies to a traveler, but he may not actually experience hardship.
2
Identify the Hanafi claim
State that the chosen (mukhtār) position is that kasr does not invalidate the 'illah. The 'illah is a sign (mazinnah) for the ruling, not the wisdom itself.
3
Examine the proof
The wisdom cannot be precisely regulated in all cases, so the Lawgiver uses a sign that is generally indicative. This sign becomes the 'illah, and the wisdom is disregarded.
Example: virginity (bikārah) is the 'illah for accepting silence as consent, even though a non-virgin may have more modesty.
4
Consider the minority objection
Some scholars argue that the 'illah is subordinate to wisdom, so any defect in the wisdom invalidates the 'illah.
Their claim: the description is only considered for the sake of the wisdom.
5
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that wisdom is only considered when it is regulable (munḍabiṭ). Since wisdom is often not regulable, the sign (mazinnah) becomes the 'illah. If wisdom were regulable, it would be used directly (as in graded punishments for different degrees of crime).

وأما الكسر وهو الحكمة تخلف الحكم عن دون العلة) التي هي المظنة كتخلف رخصة السفر عن الصنعة الشاقة في الحضر) وعند البعض الكسر يقال على النقض المكسور الذي سيجيء (فالمختار أنه لا يبطل العلية، وعليه (الأكثر خلافاً للبعض لنا العلة (المظنة لا الحكمة (وهي سالمة) لا نقض عليها (أما) المقدّمة الأولى فلأن الحكمة لما وجب اعتبارها في إناطة الحكم وامتنع اعتبار إطلاقها في تعليق الحكم به في نظر الشارع وتعذر تعيين القدر الصالح) للاعتبار بحيث ينضبط عند المكلف ضبطت بما هو أمارة (له ومظنة تيسيراً على المكلف، فتكون هذه المظنة هي المعتبرة شرعاً في إناطة الحكم فهي العلة ولغت الحكمة وما في المنهاج العلم باشتمال الوصف) المجعول علة ( عليه) أي على القدر الصالح دون العلم به أي بهذا القدر (ممتنع) فيجب علمه فحينئذ هو الحلة فأقول مندفع، لأن تعذر التعيين تحقيقاً) بحيث لا يبقى ارتياب (لا ينافي الضبط (تخميناً بما هو في الغالب مستلزم إياه (تدبر) الأقلون (قالوا: الوصف) المجعول علة (تبع للحكمة) فإنه إنما اعتبر لأجلها فهي العلة حقيقة (فالنقض) الوارد عليها وارد) على العلة فيبطل العلية (قلنا) الوصف وإن كان اعتباره لأجل الحكمة لكن لا يلزم كونها علة، بل (لا) اعتبار لها إلا إذا كانت مضبوطة) وحينئذ فالعلة لا المظنة (ألا ترى البكارة علة للاكتفاء بالسكوت في النكاح (الحكمة الحياء) لغلبته فيها والثيب ولو) كانت أوفر حياء لم يعتبر) سكوتها (إجماعاً لعدم كون مراتب الحياء مضبوطة في نفسها، بل ضبطت بالبكارة (نعم: لو كانت لها أقدار مختلفة ولكل قدر وصف ضابط مناسب لشرع حكم لا بد من تشريع) حكم أليق) بكل من الأقدار ( كالقطع) أي وجوبه (بالقطع) العمد العدوان فإنه ضابط لقدر من الجناية، وحكمه اللائق به القطع قصاصاً (تحصيلاً للزجر والقتل) أي وجوبه (بالقتل) العمد العدوان فإنه ضابط لقدر آخر من الجناية أعلى من الأول، فشرع الحكم اللائق به وهو القتل (تحصيلاً للأكثر من الزجر الموجود في الأول
And as for kasr: it is the wisdom (hikmah) lagging behind the ruling from the 'illah, which is the sign (mazinnah), like the lagging of the concession of travel behind a strenuous craft in residence. According to some, kasr is also applied to the broken refutation (naqd maksur) that will come later. The chosen position is that it does not invalidate the 'illah, and the majority hold this, contrary to some. For us: the 'illah is the sign, not the wisdom, and it is sound, no refutation applies to it. As for the first premise: because when the wisdom must be considered in attaching the ruling, but its absolute consideration in suspending the ruling is impossible in the eyes of the Lawgiver, and determining the appropriate measure that can be regulated for the legal subject is difficult, it is regulated by what is an indicator (amarah) and a sign (mazinnah) for it, facilitating for the legal subject. Thus this sign is what is legally considered in attaching the ruling; so it is the 'illah, and the wisdom is disregarded. And what is in al-Minhaj: 'Knowledge that the description (which is made the 'illah) includes it (i.e., the appropriate measure) without knowledge of that measure is impossible'—so knowledge of it is necessary and then it is the 'illah. I say: this is refutable, because the impossibility of precise determination (such that no doubt remains) does not negate regulation by estimation (takhmīn) based on what is generally indicative of it. Ponder. The minority said: 'The description made the 'illah is a follower of the wisdom, for it is considered only for its sake, so the wisdom is the true 'illah. Therefore, any refutation that applies to it applies to the 'illah and invalidates the 'illah.' We respond: Although the description is considered for the sake of the wisdom, it does not necessitate that the wisdom is the 'illah; rather, it is not considered unless it is regulated. In that case, the 'illah is the sign, not the wisdom. Do you not see that virginity (bikārah) is the 'illah for being satisfied with silence in marriage? The wisdom is modesty (ḥayā'), due to its prevalence in virgins, but a woman who has been married (thayyib), even if she has greater modesty, her silence is not considered by consensus, because the levels of modesty are not regulated in themselves; rather, they are regulated by virginity. Yes, if there were different degrees and for each degree a regulating description appropriate for legislation, then a ruling must be legislated that is most suitable for each degree, like the obligatory cutting off (for theft) by amputation (qaṭʿ) as retaliation—it regulates a certain degree of crime, and the ruling appropriate for it is amputation as retribution, to achieve deterrence; and killing (by execution) by killing as retaliation regulates a higher degree of crime, so the appropriate ruling is killing, to achieve more deterrence than the first.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage explains kasr, the defect where the wisdom (hikmah) is absent despite the 'illah being present. The author argues that the 'illah is a reliable sign (mazinnah) for the ruling, not the wisdom itself. Therefore, kasr does not invalidate the 'illah. The example of virginity and modesty illustrates: virginity is the 'illah for accepting silence as consent, even though a non-virgin may be more modest. The modesty is not regulable, so the law uses virginity as a sign. The passage also discusses an objection that the 'illah is subordinate to wisdom, but responds that only regulated wisdom matters. It ends by noting that if wisdom could be regulated, it would be used, as in the case of varying degrees of crime requiring different punishments.

Key Terms
الكسر (al-kasr): A defect where the wisdom (hikmah) lags behind the 'illah, i.e., the 'illah is present but the intended wisdom is absent.
المظنة (al-mazinnah): A reliable sign or indicator that is generally indicative of something, used as the 'illah when direct knowledge is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does kasr not invalidate the 'illah according to the majority?
A) Because the 'illah is the wisdom itself
B) Because the 'illah is a reliable sign, not the wisdom
C) Because kasr never occurs
D) Because the wisdom is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
Kasr does not invalidate the ‘illah because the ‘illah is a sign (mazinnah) for the ruling, not the wisdom itself.
